I Dr. M. PEEET,
I M.R.0.5., L.S.A , &c.,
SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Board of H.S. W. House Sur-
geon to the Great northern Hospital. London.
I am aware that I am not strictly following the paths
Of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the treatment of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Men in the treatment of
Chronic and Nervous Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph, Bov.
6th. 7th and Bth. 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified Men entered the
field to fight these pests of society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from these diseases, who are scattered over all parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider, therefore, that by advertising J not only
strike a Mow at the Quackery which now exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen iny
personal dignity.
My Qualifications 1 invite the fullest investigation into. For some considerable time 1 was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observing the mMty phases of the Nervous and
Sexual System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods ot ejecting their cure. f
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults mo he is in formed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not- The patient Is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
If I undertake the case
I Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success iu the treatment of these diseases enables me to give this guarantee without fear.

for Remedies necessary for the cure of NER-
VOU S DEBILITY. Should you require medical
treatment, as procrastination is sometimes dan-

Dr. Richards uses nothing but the purest remedies,
and never prescribes mercury under any circum-
stances. The moment letters are carefully per-
used and answered they are destroyed by fire,
and patients may rest assured that their cases
will remain a secret between the doctor and

Publications.
To he Immediately Published:
“In MIDDLE HARBOUR and other Verse;”
(Chiefly Australian)
By THOMAS HENEV.
London : Kegan, Paul, Trench, Triioner and Co
Price, Four Shillings.
In this volume, the writer has endeavoured to con-
vey his vivid impressions of the life and landscape
of the pastoral regions. Mindful of the admirable
work in such books as Mr. Farrell’s “ How Ho
Died,” and in the columns of some of the weekly
papers, the writer does not claim to have entered
an unoccupied field, but thinks it will be admitted,
by those acquainted with the scenes and persons
of his verse, that it is faithful to the virile and
picturesque characteristics of the life of the station,
and the powerful and strange scenery of pas-
toral territory. In a series of eight rhymed
poems, in three blank-verse poems, and in other
verse, it is sought to suggest the dominant features
of this free and vigorous life. In “ A Squatter of
’68,” an effort has been made to seize the dramatic
moments of the career of a pioneer squatter driven
under. ‘ ‘ Pioneer ”is an episode of the opening
of new country in South Queensland. Such poems
as “The Boundary Rider,” “ At the Tank before
Day,” “The Groom’s Hut,’’and “In the Lignum,”
are descriptive. Two poems, “The Shearer”
and “ Found Dead,” outline two of the tragedies
of the bush. In these and other verses, the writer
has endeavoured to utilise his knowledge of local
colour derived from residence and travel. The
poem from which the book gets its name is a
lengthy blank-verse study of camping-out, in
which opportunity is taken to introduce a sketch
of one of the most striking incidents of bush-life.
Of the miscellaneous verse not otherwise specified,
suffice it to say it includes ten sonnets, and
several poems of lyrical movement. The contents
of the volume are so far new that they have not
previously been published. They will make a book
of about 112 pages, which can be procured through
the leading booksellers as soon as copies are re-
ceived from London. Of the writer’s previous
book, so competent a critic as Mr. Francis Adams
wrote in warm terms in the Queenslander of
4th December, 1886, reprinted from Brisbane
Courier of immediately previous date. This
review was re-published in the most important
Riverina newspapers.

To H. F. Kugklmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 cotmilted
more than one doctor , and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but X became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
•nd of three months 1 was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, anil
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
luny healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
•Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Pkter Smvtiik, J.P.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 27, 1890.
Veneered Savages.
Someone has styled the present era, the Age
of Shoddy. The term, being apposite, has
stuck. It is, however, merely one of a
generic series, all of which may be regarded
as equally applicable. The age of adultera-
tion, for example, fits perfectly. So does the
age of stucco : and so does the age of veneer.
The potent influence of the competitive
system of social derangement has debased all
texile fabrics. Every other variety of com-
modity which permits of falsification without
instant detection, is fraudulent. The archi-
tecture of the times no less bespeaks their
character. Imposing facades simulate stone
in stucco and cement. Even when a
front is of stone, the sides and back are
ordinarily of brick, and the interior fittings
of pretentious edifices are generally mere
lath and plaster. Furniture is constructed
on similar lines. The massive sideboard, the
exquisite escritoh’e, the shining
are alike impostures. Their variegated and
gleaming surfaces thinly cover some coarser
and meaner material. Anyone with a
philosophic mind who may in later ages
disinter a nineteenth century Herculaneum
or Pompeii, will, almost certainly, arrive
by deductive reasoning at the conclusion that
the people of this epoch were themselves a
race in whose character the essential elements
of shoddy, adulteration, stucco, and veneer-
ing were deeply implanted and widely dif
fused.
•' # «
This is a speculation which to the con-
ventional thinker must appear preposterous.
The people of this great, this wonderful
nineteenth century, a race altogether super-
ficial in morals, in essential virtue, and in
the polish of humane sentiment 1 The thing
is too absurd ! Is it not this generation
which has abated warfare between any two
civilised peoples of half its terrors? Do iu.fc
the heroes of the red-cross present them
selves on every modern battle-field with thei r
ambulances ? Has not the slave-trade been
crushed ; have not privateering rights been
disclaimed by all the powers, save the
United States * Do not armies of mis-
sionaries compass sea and land to make one
proselyte, and when he is made, do they not
make him tenfold the child of —Perhaps
the bast sentence had best remain incom-
plete. When we come to think it over, the
quotation doesn’t accurately fit in with the
general argument. Curious readers will find
the entire passage in Matthew xxiii., 15.
* # #
The misadventure in the quotation line
which brought our last paragraph to an
abrupt close has somewhat disconcerted the
order of our proposed demonstration. Or
perhaps it would be more accurate to say
that the demonstration has been precipi-
tated. We may as well avow, at this
stage, that the subject most prominently
in our thoughts is the Darkest Africa
disclosure now under development. The
civilised world applauded when it learned
that Mr. Stanley, the distinguished,
the adventurous, the successful explorer,
was about to plunge once again into the
heart of Africa on a mission of rescue. The
tragic and dramatic incidents connected with
the fate of Gordon had invested the lot of
his surviving lieutenant, isolated in the
centre of Africa, hemmed in by hostile
tribes, and menaced by the victorious
dervishes, flushed with their triumph of
Khartoum, with peculiar and romantic in-
terest. The idea that a private adventurer,
with merely the equipment of an exploring
expedition, had undertaken to traverse half
of a continent, by ways untried and through
regions and races unknown, and to extricate
from the farthest and most labyrinthine
recesses of Africa, Emin Pasha, staggered
people by the sublimity of its effrontery.
The announced object stamped the expedi-
tion with a character almost holy. The
presumedly pacific nature of the party im-
parted to' the enterprise something of the
sacredness of a pilgrimage. Mr. Stanley
and companions were supposed to go forth
almost, as it were, with their scrip and their
staff, on an errand of mercy, with
‘■good will towards men” written on their
hearts. There had been nothing like
it since the fabled quest of the
Holy Grail, an enterprise which only the
pure in heart might hope to achieve. The
crusades were but gross performances, by
comparison. Everyone knew how largely
sordid and carnal objects obtruded into those
excursions. The Emin Pasha relief expedi-
tion was free from such degrading intrusions.
In its conception, the only elements were
disinterestedness, humane sentiment and
courage. No wonder that the soul of Exeter
Hall was moved within it as Mr. Stanley,
the lion-hearted, disappeared into darkest
Africa, sturdy, brown-haired, and with a
Bible clasped to his bosom !
* * *
No wonder that when this extraordinary
man emerged at the other side of the conti-
nent, after a lapse of time sufficient to excite
breathless suspense as to his fate, crowned
with success, with Emin rescued, but, alas !
himself with locks of silver, blanched by the
perils and hardships unspeakable which he
had encountered and surmounted, no wonder
that the very bowels of Exeter Hall yearned
for him, especially when he announced that
he had read the Bible three times through
during his tremendous transit. No wonder
that the very heart of humanity beat im-
pulsively as the outlines of his exploits were
told. No wonder that that gilded thing,
Society, took him to its spangled bosom,
and made of him a “lion” more popular
than Gordon Cummino, or the Shah, or
even Buffalo Bill.
» # #
But when the first clamour of acclamation
had somewhat abated, notes of discordance
were heard. It is curious, muttered some,
when you come to think of it, that Emin
should not have wanted to be rescued, after
all. It is particularly odd that the rescued
man should, as soon as he got over his acci-
dent, have just turned his back upon his
Saviour and proceeded at once to return
to the place and people he was rescued
from. Presently, too, there were whispers
that it was not Emin that Stanley had aimed
at, in reality, but a vast accumulation of
ivory which Emin had got together. Next,
an element of absurdity was introduced. If,
when the Prisoner of Chillon had emotionally
informed his hearers that “his hair was
blanched, but not with years,” and had pro-
ceeded with that remarkably touching story
of his awful dungeon experiences, some-
one had circumstantially stated that he was
really hoary-headed before he was thrown
into gaol, and came out white instead of
brown because he couldn’t get any hair-dye
when locked up, his whole narrative would
have been considerably discounted. This
was almost precisely what was affirmed with
relation to Stanley. He was a grey-haired
man when ho started, but lie was also so
much of a fop that he habitually dyed his
locks. One ot the fearful hardships he had
surmounted with the aid of his Bible was
the cowardly abandonment by an unworthy
companion of a case of hair-dye. The result
of this awful experience had been that Mr.
Stanley’s hair turned white in a single
week, and no amount of Scripture-reading
had potency to avert that sad catastrophe.
The great man’s locks were “ shoddy.”
# # #
A circumstance which sensibly qualified
and tempered the popular enthusiasm after
its first fury had been spent in England, was
the fact that national sentiment had been
jarred in Germany, and that there a whole
nation, taking Emin’s side, scoffed at Stan-
ley s pretensions. Other staggering knocks
followed. The publication of Mr. Stanley’s
ow'n book was, perhaps, the heaviest. It
was most disconcerting to discover that the
saint and almost martyr was afflicted with a
most singular insensibility and callousness
with respect to the sacred ness of human life.
That, in effect, he was quite an Old Testa-
ment hero, and not in any sense a New
Testament one. That he narrated with as
much complacency as Moses himself episodes
very similar to those recounted by the
Hebrew leader. That Stanley took it quite
as a matter of course, as did Moses, that any
people who were in his way were damned
and abandoned by their Creator, and quite
at his disposal for slaughter. The wanton
shootings, the terxific massacres, the confla-
grations, the devastations callously recorded
in his own account of his progress did not
fail to have a sobering effect evexx upon the
most exhilarated fanatic who has been in-
toxicated by the preliminary dose of Bible
and Bubble which the sagacious Stanley
had administered to the pious public of
Great Britain. Next, Paul du Chaillu
showed beyond controversion that some of
Stanley s most sensational novelties were
not new, axxd some of his most telling dis-
coveries no discoveries at all. And presently
brave, downright John Burns spoke out,
and denounced Stanley as a mere fillibuster,
whose path through Africa had been'marked
by rapine, bloodshed, and devastation.
# * *
Now come the enlightening squabbles
among the survivors of Stanley’s rear con-
tingent. A terxific beaxxx of lurid light is
shed upon this part of the true history of
this Pilgrim’s Progress. As in the opera of
“ Robert the Devil,” a sisterhood of nuns
suddenly cast off their vestnxexxts and break
out into the abandon of nxei’etxicious caper-
ixxgs, so t his band of high-purposed rescuers
fling aside their scallop-shells axxd palxxx
branches and display their true xxature as
cruel and bloody buccaxxeers. There is Bart-
telott, the chivalrous, disclosed in his real
character, fit successor to Black-beard the
Pirate. He had men flogged to death, by
uneasy stages. He kicked his little black
page so that he died. “He was,” in the
simple language of a comrade, “ a very cruel
xxxan.” Then came Mr. Jamieson. No
man of war. A plain civiliaxx. He bought
a yoxnxg girl in order to have her killed and
eatexx, for the good and sufficient reason that
he had a taste—not for her flesh, indeed—
but for Art. He wished to embellish his
collection with sketches of an act of canni-
balism, from his own pencil. He was proud
of his sketches, and showed them with conx-
placency to his comrades. It is to be
inferred that he knew whom he had to do
with. Stanley has not coixtradicted these
statements. On the contrary, after they
were made, he declared his intention of
disclosing the facts relative to the rear
detachment. What horrors can be added to
those already known it is impossible to con-
ceive. Imagination revolts from the penance
of conceiving worse things.
# * #
Thus far, however, the pxxblic have learned
only what the survivors have found it
necessary to admit with respect to the
deceased leadex’s of detachments. As it is
vain to look for the evidence of the deceased,
with respect to the conduct of the survivors,
it is only to be hoped that the latter may fall
out among themselves and testify as to what
they know of each other. There is a curious
reticence yet on the part of Stanley in
x’eference to Emin, and on the part of Emin
with respect to Stanley. Ample evidence
there is that the two men detest one
another with a rancorous hatred. Each
speaks ill and contemptuously of the other.
But neither specifies his ground of dislike,
and each mixes a sop of eulogy into the
bitter cup of his deprecation of the other.
It would be probably interesting, although
perhaps appalling, to learn all that Stanley
could toll about Emin’s proceedings, all that
Emin could disclose of Stanley’s, and all
that each could narrate of every other among
the lieutenants. Barttelott and J amieson
cannot talk back from their graves.
# # *
Sufficient, however, hxis leaked out to
illustrate the theory that the modern civilised
man is like his chattels, merely a veneered
article. Major Barttelott, in a drawing-
room, in a club, or on parade, was, we under-
stand, an ordinary type of the British officer
and gentleman. Mr. Jamieson was a citizen
who on top of an omnibus differed in no
respect from the other dozen passengei’s,
travelling of a morning to their shops and
their offices. Remove the restraints of con-
ventionality, plunge them into a region
remote from laws, endow them with
arbitx’ary power, and the gallant officer
dcvelopes into fixe ferocious freebooter—who
freeboots a small boy to death. He thrashes
a woman, tortures men horribly so that they
rot to pieces from the injuries to their flesh.
The quiet citizen in the tweed suit, with
the agreeable talent for sketching, who so
axxxiably touches off a group of your sister
and your pretty cousin when he stays at
your father’s house oxx a visit, removed to a
forest and invested with power of life and
death, hands over your sister and your pretty
cousin to his gang, to bo dealt with, because
lie has a whim to sketch their struggles, and
ho lias you flogged to death with poisoned
rods for looking glum at the transaction.
The difference is merely that the veneering
is off. But is there much room for surprise 'I
Is it not the case that daily and hourly the
entire class of reputable citizens, murder,
rob and ravish ? Is it loss murder to work
a man to death, or to worry him to death
than to flog him to death ? Are tortures
of the mind less acute than agonies of
the body ? Is a bargain which enriches
you by the impoverishment of another,
different in its result and its essence from a
highway robbery 1 Is a systematic depres-
sion of wages, which compels girls to sup-
plement their incomes on the streets, in
order to keep life in their bidies, less a
ravishment than a direct enforcement ? If
so, in what does it differ to the girl 1 Woe
to the smug complacency of the nineteenth
century Pharisee. The high civilisation he
boasts of is but as the high polish of his
dining-table. It is due to veneer. Let an
accident but strip the veneer from nineteenth
century civilisation, and in Messrs. Brown,
Jon.es and Robinson, fair average
there will be discovered Babtielotxs
Jamiesons and Stanleys.
Christmas.
Eighteen hundred and ninety years ago the
Star of Bethlehem shone out over the barren
hills of Syria, to announce that the day of a
new revelation had come upon the earth.
It was an old, weary, dying world on which
that meteor portent blazed forth—a world
that was already drifting towards chaos
under the combined influences of tyranny
and superstition, lust of conquest, incurable
licentiousness, bloodshed, vice, cowardice,
and unspeakable corruption. The old faiths
were steadily crumbling into dust, and there
was no sign of any new inspiration to take
their place. Baal and Merodach, the war-
gods of Assyria, were dead and forgotten,
and the mighty tower of Belus, on which for
1300 years the sacrificial fire had burnt in
honour of the deity of Ashur, had gone
down along with its fallen empire and its
fallen creed. Nisroch, the eagle-headed
divinity, and his sister Ashtaroth, lay buried
together in the dust of the ruined land of
Shinar ; and Isis and Osiris, Anubis,
Horus, the sacred cat, the divine onion, the
stork, and all the rest of the holy lumber of
Egypt, had been swamped together in one
general smash in the temples beside the Nile.
The Greeks, having lost their faith in their
own gods, had planted their discredited
deities throughout all Western Asia, and
treading close and fast upon their heels came
the thunder-march of Pompey, who brushed
away the new faiths as relentlessly as Cyrus
and Alexander of Macedon had brushed
away the old. Even the divinities of Rome,
however, were old and gone to seed when
they began their reign in the new empire,
and all through the East there was a mad
jumble and chaos of creeds, in which Jupiter
was mingled with faint memories of Dagon
and Baal, and Venus assimilated with the
devil-worship of Armenia, and every brick
that was thrown anywhere between Parthia
and the Atlantic was bound to smash a god,
because gods were more numerous than the
leaves of summer, or the sands by the shore
of the Great Sea. And among them all,
lonely, despised, and almost forgotten in the
little principality of Palestine, Judaism still
reared its head, and the Pharisees dashed
their maimed feet against the pavements of
Jerusalem, and cursed with a great curse at
the surrounding nations, and the Sadducees
philosophised, and the Essenes roamed
through the desert and waited for the coming
of the Redeemer who was to shatter the
legions of the Caesars and begin the world
anew.
# * *
It was a worn and crumbling world, that
sounded hollow beneath the feet of each
succeeding conqueror. Rome was already in
middle age and had lost the fire of youth;
Greece was old ; Egypt and Asia unspeak-
ably ancient in their senile wretchedness.
Justice had died with Marius, and all over
the empire the land was studded with for-
tresses where prisoners lay forgotten in sub-
terranean dungeons, and every paltry
governor kept the torture-chamber busy
and , the cross in constant requisition.
Liberty was so utterly forgotten that the
world had come to regard its chains as pare
of its very existence, and a dull reign of
terror had settled down upon the earth.
Even then the vultures were beginning, to
gather round the huge, lumbering, festering
States of the C/ksars, though it was not
until several centuries latgr that they des-
cended on the carcase ; the Parthians hung
like a thundercloud in the cast, and the sons
of Thor and Odin were whetting their
swords in the forests beside the Danube and
the Elbe. A blind, lame, halt government
of lies and frauds, of tips and swindles, and
midnight assassinations, was growing up m
place of the old government of the legions ;
the age of warriors was nearing its end, and
the ago of pimps and eunuchs was nearing
its birth, and the hand of iron which
had held civilisation together was about to
give place to (lie hand of straw and leprosy-
The men of brains—the race which had its
exemplars in Scii’if), Hannibal, and Augus-
tus —were dying out by degrees, to give
place to tho men of fat and idiocy —that


gruesome race which began with Yitellius
and culminated with Honorius, the emperor
who reared hens upon the imperial throne )
and cackled while his favourites laid eggs in
the imperial crown. It was upon this genera-
tion of slaves and worn-out voluptuaries that
the Star of Bethlehem rose, and if the
Gospel failed to make the old world new, it
was because the world to which it was given
was too old to be capable of any permanent
repair.
* # #
For a few centuries, however, it galvanised
the corpse of a dead civilisation into a
ghastly semblance of youth and energy. The
dying torch flared up with a sort of artificial
radiance, and a long Indian summer inter-
vened before the sun of the old regime set
for ever. Even the obsolete gods awoke from
Jieir long slumber on their pedestals of
gold and ivory, and for one brief period
the effete system promised to wake into
fresh activity. So long as the Gospel retained
its original purity Christianity was a living
power upon the earth, and it retained its
purity so long as the pagans continued to
burn and flay and crucify its votaries, and
no longer. The history of the faith which
came out of Galilee was short and stormy
and unspeakably tragic. But Nero died,
and Valerian died, and Julian the Apos-
tate —the noblest figure in ancient heathen-
dom—perished “ by the visitation of God,’
and the Church cursed in their graves the
imperial apostles to whose opposition it owed
not only its prosperity but its very existence.
The paganism of the Empire became extinct,
and Christianity was buried along with it in
one common grave. Constantine, the
blood-stained assassin and crowned harlequin
whom historians have deified through all
the succeeding ages, built a new gospel out
of the ruins of the old—a gospel of gold and
incense and gaudy show, of bigotry and lies
and traditions and grovelling expediency,
and straightway the incurable old age of
Jupiter and Baal descended on the new
faith, and the great Church of the East
began to totter towards its tomb.
♦ « #
The old world went its downward way
possibly a little faster under the regime of
bishops and deacons than under that of
magicians and augurs. It flayed and beheaded
the last of the pagans just as it had pre-
viously flayed and beheaded the first of the
Christians, and the alleged priest of God
was a shade more bloodthirsty and fero-
cious than the priest of Melkarth. Slavery,
which had been horrible in the days of dark-
ness, grew more horrible in the days of light,
and a cloud of unspeakable gloom brooded
over all Christendom - the gloom of murder
and human agony, of the lash and the torture-
chamber—the gloom that rose from subter-
ranean dungeons where blind and mutilated
victims lingered in eternal darkness ; while
over this festering corruption rose the shadow
of the great Church which was run by pimps
and adulterers and buffoons —a loathsome
parody on the faith that came out of Galilee.
An external varnish of fat hypocrisy and
primeval muffle was spread, like a coating of
slime, over the corpse of a dead civilisation,
and the bloated, oily man of God became
the bulwark of the empire, for the soldiers
of the new creed had little faith in their own
Paradise, and objected to die upon the
Gothic spears and depart to the bosom of
Abraham. And meanwhile the road to Rome
was trodden hard by the feet of the vengeful
tribes of Germany, and along the highways
that led out to the East towards Constanti-
nople the earth was shaken by the mighty
march of the Persian armies bent on wreak-
ing the hoarded vengeance of centuries, and
over the fields of Africa the grass shrivelled
up beneath the footsteps of the Moor, and
the Christian hid himself in his walled cities,
and drivelled, and prayed, and burned in-
cense, and walked in processions, and won-
dered why his God would not save him from
destruction. There was nothing grand or
noble about his decline. He did not end in
a blaze of glory like the warlike heathens of
Nineveh, and he made no great expiring
effort as did that mighty Pharoah who
ended the greatness of Egypt and shook
every tower and wall of Babylon in his fall.
He merely withered and corroded away, and
the Moors and the Persians slaughtered his
bishops and overturned his altars, and threw
his deacons into the sea ; and (he alleged
Christians of Germany speared his priests
and jumped on his sacred relics, and of the
old world there was nothing left save where
the crippled empire of Greece still dragged
out a precarious existence, and waited for the
Mohammedans to come and bury its dis-
honoured carcase in the tomb of its fathers.
# # #
More than half the Christian world was
lost to the faith in that great cataclysm, but
in Italy and Central Europe a now Chris-
tianity rose on the ruins of the one that was
gone. It was a larger, coarser, stronger
religion than its predecessor, and infinitely
more enduring, but beyond that its merits
were very few. The men of Germany and
Gaul kindled their camp-fires with the
lascivious literature of Rome, and smashed
pictures and statues with their battle-
ixes, and they improved on the ancient
methods, inasmuch as they fought and drank
and cursed and plundered for their creed ;
whereas the men who went before them only
lied and snuffled and served their God by
midnight assassination. The simple gospel
that arose at Bethlehem on Christmas-day,
in the first year of this era, became a
rampant gospel clad in mail, which dragged
itself through mud and massacre, and spread
peace and universal brotherhood by braining
everybody who was not sufficiently peace-
ful, and hanging everybody who declined
to be a brother. Even at the outset
of its career its moral standard was so
low that it seemed almost proof against
degeneracy, but it found means to
degenerate none the less, and the Chris-
tianity of the eighth century —however bad
it might be—was still inconceivably superior
to that of the twelfth. The world grew ripe
for another reformation, and when it began
the Church promptly proceeded to burn and
flay and impale the reformers with the zeal
which has marked every Church when upper-
most. The story which began on the star-lit
hills of Judsea was continued through many
epochs of gore and slaughter, and wherever
the foot of the Christian trod the grass
withered, and the flower faded, and the
surface of the green earth was scorched as if
by the breath of a pestilence. The hills of
Switzerland and the plains of Lombardy and
Provence were drenched with the blood of
the Waldenses and Albigenses ; the Penin-
sula was laid waste by the Inquisition ; the
Huguenots and the followers of the League
covered France with ashes and desolation,
and the reformers and the opponents of
reform were equally unpleasant in their
modes of spreading the truth as it was sup-
posed to be in Jesus. Tilly and Wallen-
stein left a track of tire through Germany ;
and the Greek Church roasted and fried
and flogged with joyous impartiality the
Catholics of Poland and the Moslems of the
Crimean khanates. The Episcopal Church
in England preached toleration, and
massacred the Catholics of Ireland and
the Presbyterians of Scotland and the
Puritans of its own country indiscriminately ;
and the Puritans of America also preached
toleration and hanged Baptists and Catholics,
and flogged Quaker women in the streets,
while all creeds and faiths and dogmas com-
bined to jump on the idolater because he
knew not the God of peace and love whose
mercy passeth all understanding.
# * *
But the storm which had raged for 18 cen-
turies dwindled down at last into compara-
tive calm. The world fought and swore
and murdered for its creeds so long as it
believed them worth the exertion, but its faith
died out in the end, and it lost the energy
necessary for acts of violence. Europe
ceased to precipitate itself upon Asia for the
recovery of the Holy Sepulchre, because it no
longer cared a straw for the Holy Sepulchre
or anything connected therewith. The Pope
abandoned the offensive habits of anathema
and excommunication, for his curse had gone
down in price till 12 of them would barely
realise a penny in the open market, and the
buyer's bill at three months would be gladly
accepted without endorsement. The “ Most
Christian King ” propped up the Vatican no
longer because the last of the Christian
Kings had escaped in a cab from the back-
door of his palace upon a day of barricades,
and the heir of the “Catholic King” raised
the banner of the Church only to be wiped
out by Concha’s bayonets and the sturdy
infantry of Moriones, and the “Defender
of the Faith ” ceased to defend any faith in
an active sense, because his subjects were
coldly indifferent to the whole subject. The
age of diseased Christianity has merged into
the age of agnosticism. The Crusade has
degenerated into the bun-struggle, the
Holy League has been born again as the
Y.M.C.A., and where the mighty figure of
Richelieu once jostled with the stars the
Rev. Job Smiffkins now dodders in the dust
with a fat cotton umbrella and a servile
smile. Gust-avus Adolphus has been taken
and the Escaped Nun is left ; and in place
of the mighty figure of Mahomet the nine-
teenth century has Joseph Smith, the in-
spired horse-thief of New England. It is a
ghastly fall compared to which even the
arrowy flight of Lucifer thrown over the
walls of Paradise was only a second-rate
gymnastic feat of very small account.
# '* *
For eighteen centuries Christianity—not
the noble Gospel of Christ, but the earthy
gospel of His alleged followers —has been
tried and found wanting. The so-called
religion of peace has been spread only by
the sword, and where the sword proved use-
less it has not been spread at all. As
the result of all those labours Europe and
America have acquired a mongrel creed
whose outward symbols are the land-syndi-
cate, and the stock-exchange swindle, the
gaol, the gallows, the public-house, the
“ ’orrid murder ” in the cheap daily print,
and the last fashionable seduction case.
Even of this crippled and mangy faith, how-
ever, three-fourths of the world’s inhabitants
know nothing. Moslemism and Buddhism
are gaining daily in the race against the
bogus Christianity which did not come out
of Nazareth, and Buddha Siddhartha has
better promise of rearing his throne in Lon-
don than has the alleged Christ— as revealed
by the modern missionary—of elevating His
sacred emblem at Benares, Except among
the European races, Christianity has gained
no disciples, and for eight centuries it has
been practically stationary. The converts of
Europe have scattered themselves over many
countries, but the great, universal redemp-
tion has not even begun, and the bright and
shining light of the Christian in foreign lands
has only served to make the Cross a thing of
loathing. Christmas Day in the year 1890
finds the cause not much further advanced
than did Christmas Day in the year One,
while its capacity for advancing is im-
measurably diminished. The fat, apoplectic
creed which goes round on its large, leaden
feet, and passes the plate, is in no condition
to undertake a Holy War, and, moreover, its
followers are not in a warlike mood. The
bang of the Salvation drum is the one sur-
viving evidence of religious activity, and
when the drummer gets old and the drum
bursts—what will remain ?

The Case- of Arthur Rae.
The deputation which waited on a certain
bowelless official, who, by a strange satire,
occupies the position of New South Wales
Minister for Justice, in regard to the case of
Arthur Rae, now a prisoner in Hay gaol for
offences against the labour-laws, appear, from
the newspaper-reports, to have not only missed
the real points of their case, but to have done
Rae’s cause much harm by introducing theore-
tical and speculative questions altogether
foreign to the issue, with which foreign ques-
tions The Bulletin does not propose to
deal. It merely intends to again put
the imprisoned man’s case before the pub-
lic in its true guise—that of an outrage
upon fair play and a challenge from monopoly
which the democracy must take up. Rae
admittedly committed two separate offences :
he acted as the Shearers’ Union agent in induc-
ing the men at work on two separate sheep-
stations to break their contracts. Accepting,
as a basis for discussion, the unholy Masters
and Servants Act, it would be equitable that,
in default of paying the fine imposed, Rae
should be incarcerated for the statutory period
- — i. e., for 14 days in each case, or (the punish-
ment in each case being made cumulative) 28
days in all. But the magistrate decided that
Rae should go to gaol, not for two fortnights
merely, but for a separate fortnight in regard
to every man who had come out in obedience to
the Union mandate or suggestion of which
defendant was the admitted bearer. (The fact
that the alternative was reduced from a fort-
night to a week in the second lot of cases in no
way affects The Bulletin’s argument.) Suppose
Rae had been a bushranger, and had, pistol in
hand, stuck up all the people on the two stations
on one day, or on two separate days, with what
would he have been charged ? Simply with
having committed two acts of highway robbery
under arms. Suppose he had robbed two
squatters of a sovereign each, he would, we
presume, have been arraigned for having
stolen two separate sums of one pound each
—-every one will, we trust, readily believe
that he would not have been sentenced
to a separate term of imprisonment on
account of each of the 240 separate pence of
which each pound was composed. Rae is now
undergoing 128 weeks’ gaol for what we
contend are but two, and not seventy or
eighty, separate offences. Metaphorically
speaking, an old English capitalistic Iuav
is exacting from him its pound of flesh for
each of the pence contained in the pound in
dispute. Mr. Gould is “considering” the
matter. If he allows Rae to remain in gaol for
one single moment over the two fortnights
which, the present labour laws being adminis
tered fairly, Rae is liable to serve, he asserts in
presence of the workmen of Australia the prin-
ciple that a mere magistrate may summarily
send to gaol for two thousand fortnights—
seventy or eighty years—a man who has con-
veyed to a body of one thousand workmen an
intimation that they had better strike ! The
wage-earners of Australia don’t thank the weak
and trimming lawyerette who has been foisted
into a position which should be filled by a
strong and manly man, for his insulting intima-
tion that the term of imprisonment passed upon
Arthur Rae is “ perhaps somewhat excessive.”
On the contrary, they regard that intimation as
a blasphemy against Labour and a travesty on
Justice.

Parnellism and Snuffle.
Parnell is fighting an uphill battle against all
the combined forces of British cant and snuffle-
dom, and considering the size of his adversaries
the probabilities are that he will lose. The
results, so far, show that the uncrowned king
has made a number of mistakes that are almost
irreparable. In the first place, he went astray
with a woman of education and refinement,
instead of lying in wait for a little girl round a
dark corner, and then casting his victim away.
Apparently, too, he went astray for the un-
accountable reason that he and the lady who
shared his fall were devotedly attached to each
other, and this circumstance is beyond the com-
prehension of the great world of snuffledom ;
and he allowed judgment to go by default
because he was willing to swap his outward
respectability, and the opinion of his fellow-
men, and the approval of the parson and the
landlord and the fat capitalist, for the sake of
a woman, and nothing more. And on top of all
his other misdoings, he defrauded the moral
press out of some thirty or forty columns of
immoral details, and consequently many
“special editions ” were lost, and the chance of
dragging Mrs. O’Shea’s name through a vast
amount of festering sensation never came off.
Lastly, having resigned his position as leader
of the Irish party, and been unanimously
re-elected, he has refused to resign over
again, and has tacitly defied his critics,
But the attitude assumed by Gladstone
and Morley has scared the Irish party
into a desire to re-consider its position, and if
the choice should ultimately be between Ireland
and Parnell, no doubt Parnell, as the smaller
of the two, will have to go overboard. Yet it
is worth while considering that if the “un-
crowned king ” is to be ejected from his position
for his moral deficiencies, it would be safe to
eject an enormous proportion of the House of
Commons on the same charge. Immorality, in
general terms, is a joke and a pastime; im-
morality when it comes down to particulars, is
a crime. Immorality that is squared by a
cheque amounts to nothing, but immorality that
has not been squared by a cheque is ruin. The
seduction of a score of peasant girls would have
left either Parnell or Dilke where he stood
before ; but the seduction of a lady connected
with “society” stands on a different basis.
The latter may be conspicuously and almost
openly the favourite of a prince, but to be the
favourite of a commoner is absolutely fatal. In
the case of the one it is practically a matter of
no account whether he is found out or not; as
for the other he may be found out in the
ordinary sense of the word with complete im-
punity, but to be found out officially is a
destructive circumstance. And the one thing
which cannot be found out under any possible
conditions is the moral law itself, for it is an
intangible and unfixable thing that passes all
human comprehension.
A Departmental Shadow.
The Victorian Railway Department has got
left on a cold day through the researches of the
new standing committee on Public Works. The
committee wanted to know, and the depart-
ment looked at it with a bewildered glare and
said it really couldn’t tell. It acknowledged,
however, that it paid some £290 a mile for
fencing, and other people got the same kind of
fencing done for £l2O, but it had no distinct
idea of the reason, and didn’t see any earthly
prospect of ever finding out. When it bought
land that was offered in the open market for
£3 an acre, it generally gave about £4O, but it
supposed this was because people usually asked
more from the Government than from anybody
else. Naturally enough the committee wanted
to know why the department didn’t put its
great flat foot down on this swindle, but the
department didn’t know; and when the com-
mittee further demanded whether any member
of the department would stand such a thing if
he were buying land on his own account, the
department didn’t think it at all probable.
Being asked if it were aware that it drew its
salary for looking after the public interest, it
admitted vacantly that it had never looked
at the matter in that light before. The
department generally paid Is. 3d. per cubic
yard for earthwork, while the ordinary price
charged by small contractors was Bd., but
it was pointed out that if small contracts were
let it would involve much more “supervision.”
It was the same kind of earth in each case, but
it was generally understood that earth at eight-
pence required to be looked at harder than
earth at Is 3d, although the actual physical
exertion of looking at eight coppers was much
the same as the strain of looking at fifteenpence.
When a contractor had finished a line, the de-
partment had generally to pay £20,000 or so
for putting it in working order, but they paid
the contractor just the same. There had been
sixteen refrigerating cars built for the convey-
ance of butter, and meanwhile the butter was
being carried in open trucks while the re-
frigerating cars were used for the conveyance of
oats, but whether oats wanted to be refrigerated
the department didn’t know. The principal
boss engineer said that if the line to Bruthen
was built on the narrow-gauge principle it
would save £lll,OOO, and the principal survey-
ing gaffer said it would only save £15,000.
They didn’t think the discrepancy was worth
arguing about, anyhow, and the leading pam-
pered menial of the construction branch was of
the same opinion. One of them had guessed
his figures, and the other fellow couldn’t recol-
lect how he arrived at his, but he thought they
grew upon him by a gradual process, like dirt or
moss or barnacles, and then a third man came
in and struck a general average out of his head.
Next the committee asked for some information
about a bridge that was being built over a river
somewhere, but the leading officer of the
bridge department had never heard of it, and
when he was asked if it was true that it would
block navigation, he stated regretfully that he
didn’t know; and when he was asked if the
local inhabitants were raising Gehenna over it,
he remarked that he couldn’t tell. The rest of
the witnesses were even more impalpable, and
when the chairman rose at last in uncon-
trollable indignation, and demanded whether
anything in the whole department was what it
seemed to be, or whether everything was
really something else, the testifier then before
the court only hauled up his collar and
vacantly replied that it was, and being asked
what it was, he lighted up with a great, glad
smile of intelligence, and answered “Yes.”
The horrible indefiniteness of the railway office
had infected everybody right down to the char-
woman, and none of them knew anything.

Concerning' a Commission.
The first great principle of statesmanship is to
get its salary raised, and the second is to shove
the responsibility on to somebody else, and
when, here and there, a statesman appears who
is possessed of three principles, the third one
consists in lying low and not committing him-
self. Consequently, whenever a great social or
political problem arises, it is the business of the
politician to make a stupendous but unavailing
effort to deal with it, and at the same time to
be prevented by unforeseen circumstances from
ever doing so. The problem of Capital and
Labour is the snag ahead in modern politics,
and as the most convenient way of rushing
boldly at the difficulty and never getting there
the Parkes Government has appointed a mild
and respectable Commission to shove back the
sea and hold up the deluge. Its business is to
inquire into the cause of strikes in the past and
find means to prevent them in the future, and
it consists principally of plain, fat, dull indi-
viduals, whose ideas are limited to orthodoxy
and prayer, and the sanctity of property, and
the necessity for doing nothing contrary to the
habits and customs of their forefathers. It is
filled with the solemn, commonplace spirit of
the retail tailor and the undertaker, and the
purpose of its existence is to find out how an
irresistible force may crash up against an im-
movable object without doing any damage or
creating a row. The capitalist owns the earth,


Christinas is hero ; you’ll want it badly.
Now is the time to lay in a supply. Fortified by
Mar hall’s Tonic Nervine yon may face undaunt-
edly any home-made pie.

have much swollen bank-balances, and vastly-
increased sorrows throxxgh axx over-acquaintance

On Statues and Other Thing’s.
The London Echo xxxakes a small, plaixx, harm-
less observation:—
Major-General Bland Strange, who has been travelling-
leisurely in Australia, and taking note of everything,
quotes some elegant expressions from the Australian
Separatist journals. The Bulletin calls Gordon “ a
sainted nigger-butcher.” On the other hand, the people
of Melbourne raise a fine statue to this “ outcher.” So
that opinions differ—luckily. Again, the name of the
Hon. Mr. Dalley is pretty well known in England. The
Dalley memorial in Westminster Abbey is the first ever
placed there in honour of a colonial statesman. But Tub
Bulletin suggested that a more appropriate monument
would be the figure of an ass in a lion’s skin.
The Bulletin rises with a hollow-sounding and
mysterious cough to make one remark by way
of reply —namely, that a statxxe is no more a
guarantee of any man’s character than is a
porous-plaster, and a memorial in Westminster
Abbey is not a proof that Dalley was a great
patriot and a great statesman —it would be as
reasonable to claim that a shattered hat and a
copious stream of profaxxity wex-e eA 7 idences of a
profound kxxowledge of astronomy. The man
who gets a statue is sometimes a genius and a
hero, but more often he is not; the man who
gets ten statues is generally a fool. Columbus,
for instance, is somewhat badly off in -this way,
while Albert the Good absohxtely cumbers the
leaxx earth with busts, monuments, and pede-
stals. That dismal buffoon, William the
Fourth, shines considerably, so far as images
are concerned, in various British cities, but
William Wallace and Daniel O’Connell are
somewhat scarce. Brown, boot-polisher to the
Royal Family, is a good deal more conspicuous
than Robert Bruce or Patrick Sarsfield,
but any one who would judge from that fact
that Brown was a greater man than the heroes
of Bannockburn or Limerick had better hie
away to a lunatic-asylum before his disorder
becomes incurable. All the cities of Syria were
once choke-full of statues to Antxochxls Ex’i-
i’HANES, even in the days when that howling
drunkard used to gallop through the streets
disguised as a wild beast, with his harem rush-
ing after him and holding up his tail. A statue
is only a solid evidence that somebody has beexx
fool enough to put it up, anti a tablet in an
Abbey is merely a proof that somebody placed
it there. It doesn’t even prove that the pex'son
to whom it was erected had any existence, for
even the Devil has had statues raised to him,
and the world doesn’t feel quite so positive
about him as it used to be.
The Austral Salon has, with the customary
modesty of the female sex, enunciated as a
satisfactorily-demonstrated canon the principle
that, morally, man is lamentably inferior to
woman. We have not been favoured with a
statistical analysis of the processes through
which this conclusion has been arrived at, but
assuming the correctness of the assertion from
an abstract point of view, it is certainly incon-
trovertible that, practically regarded, man is
in nine cases out of ten more reliable in his
actions than the best of women. The reason is
obvious. The efficiency of a machine is in
proportion to its simplicity, and granted that a
woman be fundamentally more conscientious
than the avex’age male person, the depreciating
circumstance still remains that the complexity
of female character will usually render her
actions indecisive and irregular, whereas that
of man being based on one simple principle—
i.e., ambition or greed—call it whichever may
be preferred—gives him the superiority in
chances of success owing to his lack of suscepti-
bility to influence by alternations of circum-
stances or motives. The vexy multiplicity of
women’s virtues renders their conduct vacillating
and imperfect, and the results achieved nuga-
tory.

BJ6 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Dec. 22, 1890,
My Dear Moorabinda, —
The worm is indeed i’ the bud when women begin to
publicly make nasty remarks about the clergy, and it is
ominous that in a crowd of letters, mostly complaining
about the workers in the vineyard, in the S.M. Herald,
some of the most cruel are from lady correspondents. The
leading objection that feminine Sydney seems to have to
its Chadband, is that he doesn’t call frequently enough—
doesn’t appear smiling at the back-entrance on washing#
days, nor attach himself violently to the knocker when
there is an invalid in the front room, as he used to in the
old, happy past. It seems that he has contracted a
pernicious habit of waiting to be called upon, which has
alienated the faith of several earnest sisters who fail to
appreciate the inwardness of a coy curate with a fascina-
ting clean shave when he is only visible in his own
drawing-room, surrounded by a covey of young women
who want the first chapter of Genesis explained to them ;
and unless the parson doesn’t re-commence his visitations,
the Church will have to stand from under—
The parson has given up calling,
And the ladies don’t want to have wings,
Where’s the point about Sheol appalling
(As the hymnaFso frequently sings)
If you’re shown all the “ bogies ” in chapel
With the choristers waiting to shout ?
Where’s the blush over Eve and the apple
With no audience hanging about?
What’s the good if you shudder at Tophet
Alone in your poker-backed pew
AVithout the limp hug of the prophet
To infer that it isn’t for “yew?”
Where’s the point in your bogus “ defection ”
If the shepherd don’t weep o’er your fall ?
They’re noticing in the collection
That the parson’s forgotten to call.
It strikes me that the cleric and the medico—celestial
and terrestial healers, as Shakespeare calls them—run
very much on the same principles right along. The
doctor who diagnosed the oases of all bis beauteous lady
patients at the same time in his consulting-room would
either have to mend his ways or take in his lamp ; and
the parson who tries to round up his lost lady souls in a
mob instead of saving them at one time in shady drawing-
rooms while hubby is down town will be sure to find one
shirt-button and a “Tommy-dodd” sixpence the full
extent of the Sunday collection.
“ When walking in the Gardens on a Sunday morning,
about the hour of 11,” remarks one of my beloved sisters
in a morning paper, “it is pleasant and memory-stirring
to hear the sound of voices singing hymns floating acros s
the bay from H.M.S. Orlando; perhaps it is still more
interesting to go on board and participate in the service,
as a number of ladies did on last Sunday, when the con-
gregation included’’—here follows a long list of fashion-
ables. How interesting and romantic ! So far as I have
heard, the Orlando, like all the rest of the Fleet, has
hitherto been regarded by society simply as a dance-room,
and it is rather sudden and comforting to now find her
viewed as a church. But I am inclined to suspect that the
fashionable creatures who use the flagship as a scene of
devotion are induced to go there from a variety of
harmless motives besides piety,. Whenever in future
I see naval-officers sitting-out their dances behind the
screen of waving flags, or meet “ Jack Muck” tacking up
George-street with a convoy of muslin on each arm, I
shall be reminded of how “pleasant and memory-
stirring ” it is to go to church on the Orlando on a Sun-
day morning. Most assuredly the very nearest approach
to Heaven that a good-looking woman can make by land
or sea is the morning service on board of a man-of-war as
a prelude to—er—looking over the albums—and other
social vanities—in the ward-room.
People who continually hunger and thirst after the
latest p’ints about etiquette now want to know
whether it is correct for a married lady to sign her own
initial—as “Mrs. S. Jones” when her husband’s name is
Thomas? Up to date it was only correct for a widow to
display her own letter in this way, and they are saying
that the Sydney “ society ” matron who recently wrote
herself down thuswise either intends it as a hint to her
lawful hubby to—ah, mature his life-policies, or else
doesn’t know enough about the hanky-panky of polite
society to be trusted to dog-ear her own visiting cards.
I am told that the Hon. Misses Estelle and Dorothea
Hope, sisters of Governor Hopetoun, debuted in public at
Melbourne Town Hall last Tuesday afternoon, at the
speech-day of Methodist Ladies’ College. They are
exactly alike, and Connie Gilchrist’s carte-de-visite would
serve for both. They have “blonder” hair even than
their illustrious brother. Not a speck of fashion about
them, but they turned up in their almost dowdy pale-blue
morning-gowns, as on ship-board, with little crape hats,
trimmed neatly with small red and white roses and leaves.
The interesting and infrequent conversazione held once
in every two years by the N.S.AV. Royal Society (why
“ Royal” I do not know, since British Royalty,' far from
being scientific has so far as I can read and hear, ever
been characterised by its addiction to coarse amuse-
ments to the exclusion of everything scientific), occurred
in the great hall of the ’Varsity at 8.30 p.m., on 10th
December. The attendance was a crush The main hall
was set with exhibit tables, mostly adorned with micro-
scopes arranged over trichina), liver-fluke, and other airy
trifles, and astronomical, bibliological, bacteriological,and
I don’t know how many more ’ogical specimens were
strewn around in a reckless profusion which brought
gleams of rapture to the far-off eyes of long-haired learned
men, most of whom combined business with pleasure and
brought their note-books along. Undoubtedly the draw
of the evening was Mr. Government Analyst Hamlet s
micro-spectroscope with which he detects blood-stains on
murderers’ clothing, and discovers what the last suicide
had for dinner and whether it agreed with him ; and the
way all the women shuddered at the very idea, and yet
individually applied a bright blue, brown, or bilious eye
to the small round glass at the top shows what a satisfac-
tion the morbid has for our common humanity. The
grounds were lit up with hosts of little coloured lamps,
and the laboratories - also thrown open to the guests with
practical demonstrators laid on for explanatory purposes
—were brilliant with Chinese lanterns. In the great hall
groups of palms and sombre-coloured furniture were the
only adornment—the penny-fan and paper-banner of the
remorseless “art’’-decorator not being allowed to spoil
the old brown walls. A daintily-supplied buffet across
the top of the hall showed that even scientists sometimes
feel the inwardness of coffee and ices.
“Everybody” was “there,” of course; what wmuld
not people do just now to get away from their
own society just for one blissful evening?—and the throng
was received by about six leading lights of the Society at
the eastern door, the names being announced by a hire-
ling. Lord Charles Scott (just back from a happy day in
the country) and Lady Scott, Sir Henry Parkes, on a
stick, with a dazzling display of “stars” and “orders”
and red and blue ribbons, probably donned to keep people
from noticing that his hair w'anted combing, and scores of
other notabilities, including apparently all the judges,
swelled the throng, which became rather too numerous
for the ventilation when everybody had come. As for
frocks, Lady Scott w’ore a handsome shell-pink surah,
high bodice, and train over a front of bouffant-pink striped
crepe, the upper portions of the sleeves of fine white lace,
the bodice finished at the neck with lace and folds of pale-
pink crepe. Mrs. B. B. Wise wore white silk and striped
gauze, white fan ; Mrs. Mitchell, old-gold satin and fire-fly
passementerie ; Mrs. Yanderveer Green, white corded silk
with front of crystal tulle. Mrs. (Judge) Backhouse,
pearl-grey moire and caruhia silk, cluster of ostrich-
feathers on the left shoulder; Miss Wilkinson, pale
yellow silk and chiffon frills ; MissGarran, yellow' silk and
fisher’s net; Mrs. (Dr.) Shewen, grey silk and gauze and
point lace. A plurality Of black gowns lent their dismal
presence to the scene, among the wearers of this useful
depressing hue being Mrs. Cecil Darley, in silk and lace ;
Miss Mary Martin, in silk tulle and jet lit up by a cluster
of pink pelargoniums ; Mrs. Garran and Mrs. Gurney.
Have you ever noticed how 7 strangely most people
behave on being announced into a large assembly ? They
breathe their precious names to the butler in a husky
whisper, as if they felt sure the whole company would go
away if they knew 7 who was the aw 7 ful criminal effecting
an entrance, and when the painstaking helot has hoarsely
announced “Mr. Bones” for Mr. Jones, Mr. Jones doesn’t
walk, but shies in, looking like a lost soul getting into
Paradise on a press-ticket and expecting to find it expired.
The positive rudeness of these embarrassed souls towards
their entertainers would be dreadful if it were not so
pitiful. I have seen women announced—mild, pleasant
creatures in every-day life—who tipped up their chins
with a haughty stare, not even returning the bow 7 of their
host and hostess. Girls who crowd nervously in in the
wake of a suave mamma sometimes negotiate the ordeal
with agonised eyes fixed on their welcomer as if he were
going to bite them, while others keep their eyes impolitely
on the floor as they zig-zag through the doorway. And
then the mistakes in the names !—the parties of three
who give tw 7 o baptismals to the butler ! It is a very
strange, Mormonish sort of thing to hear “ Mrs. and Mrs.
Thingumbob” loudly announced, and then to see a man
srail ngly appear, herding up two women.
The “ ladies concert,” held in Sydney Centennial Hall,
on 11th inst., for the purpose of reimbursing those dis-
consolate enthusiasts who, after rashly going bail for
local musical culture, discovered, at the end of the
orchestral series, that a £2OO deficit remained to be
squared—was a well-attended, dressy affair which realised
something over the required total. Nearly everybody
was in full ceremonial rig, and the spectacle of Lord
Precedence Scott sitting peacefully side by side with
Mayor Burdekin in the fauteils, lent quite a millennial air
to the glittering front row. Lady Charles Scott wore
amber silk and chiffon with a painted fan ; Miss Ryan
(her sister), ciel blue ; Mrs. Burdekin, pale-blue
silk and chiffon ; Miss Burdekin, electric grey with
corselet of darker velvet ; Miss Broomfield, grey and
cardinal touches ; Mrs. Walter Hill, black, lighted with
superb diamonds ; Miss Burdett-Smith, pale-pink surah >
and so on, ad infinitum —the satisfactory state of the
reserved seats being more largely due to the exertions of
the comprehensive committee of 163 ladies than to local
passion for melody at five shillings a time. The eleventh-
hour defection of Signora Link alone excepted, the con-
cert eventuated as per programme. Mrs. Yanderveer
Greene, the only lady soloist, sang Randegger’s “Slumber
Song ” in an extra sleepy, though artistic fashion, and two
special floral baskets w 7 ere presented at the finish (ori-
ginally intended, possibly, for the missing link) to the
charming Knickerbocker warbler, whose filmy frock of
cream tulle, gold sequins, and saffron roses became her to
a T.
Apropos to ladies’ committees—an indignant whisper is
going up that many of the women who are foremost in
the proceedings (on paper), their names high on
the list, let their efforts in the good cause cease with that,
while they talk loudly of “ our success ” if the show turns
up trumps. The glory attached to hanging on to a
“ committee ” is rather thin, but an advertisement is an
advertisement, and the energetic workers attached to
some of the fashionable affairs seem to think that the
drones who write themselves down and nothing more,
obtain “honor” under false pretences and reap what
others have sown. I hear that a sort of secret society—
to be furnished with the names of all “ professional com-
mittee ladies is meditated, to prevent them in future
from having an idle finger in the pie. What a storm in a
tea-cup, isn’t it ?”
The latest suggestion from the spasmodic (esthetics of
Sydney is-in brief—that a dozen society dames should be
<< at home”—one at a time—to their friends in the local
Botanical Gardens once a week, so that by-and-by the
general public would become so embued with the habit of
standing round and watching Mrs. de Potts Point
“receive” that it would come mechanically of its own
accord, and thus a pleasing custom would be estab-
lished to last all summer. The hygienic advantages that
would accrue to society pets who received or paid their
calls in the hot weather in the Gardens are also ex.
patiated upon, and the article closes, so to speak, with
three cheers Dr the cause. All of which is Arcadian, but
impossible. For one thing, “society” doesn’t pay calls
in the summer ; it either flees into the wilderness or lurks
in the coal-celiar with all the front blinds down until the
chilblain season dawneth. And in the winter the weather
is too erratic for anybody to vaguely announce that she
would be “at home” in the “Gardens,” without the
addition of “galoshes and umbrellas provided.” But no
amount of pleading will make “doing the Gardens”
fash’onable. Years ago “nice” people used to go and
hear the band play, but the company on the lawn became
too assorted even for Sydney, and the thing fell through
and has remained through ever since.
Miss Kate Pearce, of Tasmania, is responsible for a very
lively schottisohe, evolved under the dreary title of the
“ Mount Bischoff,” and rendered still more depressing by
a balloon-view of the mine, crowned with the photo, of a
patriarchial party named Smith, who is patron-saint of
the dotted semi-quavers within. Miss Pearce has kept up
the beat well right through, and the music is very
danceable, but the printing (done in Launceston) is
execrable.
I wonder when musicians will write waltz-music
straight out, without worrying about the “ intro-
duction?” Every waltz has a page of introductory
squeaks, which everybody completely ignores, and unless
it’s just for the “ look of the thing ” that composers write
bars of preliminary melody, they might just as well save
themselves the trouble. For nobody ever dreams of
taking any notice of these premonitory symptoms of the
valse, and even bands at public balls dash into the air
straight out, without tootling through a sort of semi-
“ piece ” before getting to business. The “ introduction”
is an interesting fossil, which flourished at a time when a
promenade round was part of the excitement of the dance,
but now when each couple plunges away at the sound of
the music, the musician who started out on the “ prelude ’
in common time to “No. 8, a Waltz,” would witness the
horrible sight of a number of people rotating with a pained
expression to four crotchets in the bar. Therefore,
nobody plays the introduction.
Speaking of waltzes reminds me that at one of the
recent Sydney public balls somebody picked up a
“scapular,” or Agnes Dei, in the gentlemen’s ’tiring'
room. An Agnes Dei—l may explain for the benefit of
the unorthodox —is a sort of holy charm to protect the
wearer from external evil influences, and the fact of a
man wearing his to a ball proved that he had been there
before, and knew where his little talisman would come
out strongest. It would be interesting to know how he
felt when his safe-guard slipped—whether his moral
resolutions went by the board, so to speak, or whether he
kept from spooning the fluffy blondes just as austerely
without it.
Quite the thrilling event of the socially hopeless week
before Christmas was the Sydney Liedertafel concert on
18th inst., in the expansive Centennial Hall. The great
throng was headed by Mrs. Burdekin, and finished off by
a small, tan-coloured tyke which sat on its tail in the
eastern gallery and tapered-off the crowd gloriously.
There was—speaking generally—no dressing, only the
sorry crushed nun’s-veiling gown, the flotsam of the
season fled, in colour grey, pinks, cream or brown,
adorned each she from heel to head. And some had
sleeves, some shoulder-straps, some arms were red, some
thin, somefat. Butveiling was the only wear—your Sappho
answers for that! The concert itself was slightly
handicapped at first by a wild, Liedertafelian instinct to
transpose the original score half-a-tone lower (which is a
nice way of saying that the tenori were as flat as pan-
cakes in the opening number), but they looked
so artistic in their boiled shirt-fronts, with all the vests cut
according to distinct sartorial idiosyncrasies, against a
back-ground of potted plants that one readily forgave
their weird preliminary. “King Olaf’s Xmas” was the
Liedertafel’s special effort, since it was so apropos to the
season and so vigorously rendered. Signora Link, Miss
Lottie Hyam, and Lucy Alltress were the extraneous
talent introduced to lend variety to the programme—on
which the management had wisely marked an “ interval ”
before the concluding item to give the iron-shod suburban
barbarian a chance to catch its precious tram without
disturbing the rest of the company. Signora Link, in a
pink silk gown draped in designed pink chiffon, sang the
“ Jewel Song” from “ Faust” like a syrup, and if she only
wouldn’t agitate as if she were bouncing a cradle when
the tempo begins to tear its hair your Sappho would give
her a hall-marked blessing as a Christmas-box.
There was no special “fashionable” frenzy over the
return of Mister Toole to Sydney Royal, on 19th inst., and
the capricious d.o. was patchy. A strawberry blonde with
her blazing thatch frizzed out in all directions like askye-
terrier oast a warm glow over the front chairs, and the
one “ sassiety ” person I saw present relied principally on
the amber-silk lining of her opera-wrap for effect. The
small comedian opened in “Paul Pry” and “Off the
Line,” and was supported by the same galaxy of youth
and beauty. Irene Vanburgh in a cream frock cut d la
Dickens—all the wardrobe in “ Paul Pry ”is Dickenesque
—looked younger than ever, the chokey quality of
Lownde’s unmusical voice made one think he was con-
tinuing to feel sea-ill, and Eliza Johnstone, Toole, and
Billington were the geniuses to whom the house accorded
special welcome. On 21st inst. (Sunday) the Toole com-
pany spent a ’appy day on the ’arbour.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Imperial Faderationists claim to have won
over to their views many Victorian Native back-
sliders. The medium of this victory is one Taylor
a somewhat flatulent bank-clerk—who holds
nations in the hollow of his brain.
Is it true that Farnell, convicted at Sydney
of forging a bill on his mother and sent to prison,
is serving his term like anyone unpossessed of
political influence ? Or, on the other hand, has he
had a lot of time remitted and been released ?
Or, is he about to be released ?
“ The respondent always looked very
drinky,” said a witness the other day in the Mel-
bourne Divorce Court. A handy adjective of
this kind has long been wanted in general circula-
tion. Didn’t Mary Lamb once write : “Charles
came home last night very drinky and smoky ” ?
The Equitable Life Assurance Society of
the United States, Sydney, has just received a
cable from their head office at New York, stating
that the Society’s architect will start for Sydney
about 25th inst., with a view to commencing
operations in connection with proposed new build-
ings in Sydney and Melbourne.

Sydney Garrick is a pretty little theatre,
nicely, not gorgeously furnished, of about the
same seating-capacity as the Criterion, and suit-
able for the production of the same style of plays,
Mr. W. J. Wilson, who, with Fred. Hiscocks, is
lessee, has painted a very pretty little drop-scene
(a view of the Lago D’Orta) and everything,
position, building and appointments, bespeak
success for the Garrick and its management.
Their chief difficulty will be the procuring of com-
panies to play the sort of pieces suited to the
house and the class of people that may reasonably
be expected to attend it. However, they opened
the theatre on Monday (Dec. 22) with “Moths.”
an adaptation of Ouida’s novel by Henry Hamil-
ton, interpreted by a really first-class company
from the stable of W, and G., to wit, Chas.
Cartwright, Olga Nethersole and Nellie Lyons,
all of whom appear for the first time in Australia,
Laurence Cautley, Edward Sass, Hans Phillips,
Jenny Watt-Tanner, Emma Bronton, and Emma
Gwynne
* * *
On Saturday night last William Rignold,
who went home by the Austral, was “ benefited ”
by a large attendance at Sydney Her Majesty’s
when the “ Merry Wives of Windsor” was played
for the last time, and his portrait, as Falstaff, was
presented to everyone as a souvenir of the occa-
sion. The mighty William, who is a universal
favourite, in front and behind, promises to return
at an early date with some novelties. This house
closed on Monday and Tuesday, and will be
opened on Christmas Eve with “ Dick Whitting-
ton and His Cat,” when Maggie Moore, the plump,
genial, pathetic and witty, will make her first
appearance in pantomime in Sydney. Moreover,
in the first pant, ever played at this house.
** * *
It is no new thing for the management of
Sydney Criterion to “take off” a piece ih the
height of its popularity. “Dr. Bill,” who hangs
out his brass plate for the last time on Monday
night, was, apparently, good for several weeks
longer. Right through up to the last night, it
was impossible to make sure of seats unless they
were secured beforehand, and Myra Kemble may
confidently reckon on getting another Sydney turn
out of it. B. C. Stephenson’s play, “Impulse,”
will _be staged on Boxing Night, when Maud
Williamson will make her first Sydney appearance,
and Geo. Titheradge, Fanny Enson, Percy Lyndal
and other favourites will reappear.
** * *
The gap existing between Potter and
pantomime at Sydney Royal has been made in-
teresting, chiefly by reason of its misadventures.
First, Laurence Cantley, Courtly, Cautely, Cautley
(the Sydney press is lavish of names for him) was
laid up with relaxation of the throat, and Robert
Inman played Denver for two nights with a
hunk of manuscript in his hand, despite which,
he displayed some capacity for playing the
part. On these occasions Hambro played
Geoffrey Ware very well indeed. “ The
king ” had no sooner recovered than his “missus,”
Emma Bronton, took seriously ill one evening,
while getting ready to go on. Maggie Moore was
sent for and arrived at the theatre only a few
minutes prior to the raising of the curtain. The
little woman, who played the part for a month
about five years ago, when Annie Mayor had been
disabled by an accident at Katoomba, got through
like a workman, without hitch or stumble, and
gave every line its due value. The only flaw in
her performance was the over-vigour with which
she defied Coombes in the hut-scene. It suggested
a randy, not a lady in reduced circumstances. (As
Emma Bronton did not recover, Maggie played the
part on the last two nights of the advertised season,
as also on the evening when Comedian Toole,
delayed by head-winds, arrived too late from New
Zealand, and grievously disappointed a number of
fat aldermen waiting to interview him, who did
not neglect, however, to interview the fat supper
intended for him. Toole, who will probably end his
career as asuburban mayor, madehis re-appearance
on Friday in “ Paul Pry ” and “Off the Line,” two
of his best performances up to date, though both
pieces were played in a rather flabby manner.
New Zealand beef and beer seems to agree with
the company, who seem much improved, physi-
cally, but it is difficult to say what would improve
them histrionically. The Toole season lasts, with
the aid of “Dearer than Life,” etc., till Christmas
Eve, and “Cinderella” will shove on the glass
slipper on Boxing Night.
** * *
Melbourne humanitarians are up in arms
because an expert says the poor alligator in the
aquarium is wasting and pining away for the want
of live fowls, kittens, and puppy dogs, the open-
countenanced chap being like Lydia Languish in
the play, when she refused to have a marriage with-
out an elopement—in other words, he must have
live game, kill it himself, and get no cagmag of
old steak. But what a fine old tearful crocodile is
dear Sister Grundy, who chucks the infanticided
kids to the three-headed dog Cerberus, guarding
the gates of Tophet, where Satan jobs them with
his spiky trident for all eternity, and serve the
wicked little devils right.
** * *
Things go on much the same as usual at
Sydney Cyclorama, except that the old man who
fought at Gettysburg has not so much room to
point out to his grandchildren the tree behind
which he dodged the bullets on that memorable
occasion, nor has the lecturer so much time to
apologise to him for the unintentional omission of
his classic mug from the canvas.
