The only Wyburd has engaged, we are
told, a full minstrel company for Bondi aquarium
at this joyful period, and numerous other enter-
tainments will be given. At his King-street hall,
the Circassian youth, who sings and fiddles
divinely, Henier's orchestra, and Lilian, the child-
thot, are billed.
The shadow of approaching Christmas has
darkled (new word) over Melbourne shows these
last few days, and a large proportion of public
money has drifted from the drama to be invested
in the fatted goose. At the Opera House, how-
ever, the story of Madame Angot’s daughter has
been cordially received by admirers of that smart
game chicken, whilst “The Squire”—a much
stronger performance that of two years ago—kept
the Bijou comfortably filled all last week.
“ Madame Angot ” runs on until Christmas Eve,
but the Brough-Bouoicault production gracefully
withdrew itself after Saturday, leaving the Bijou
to the mercy of two unprofessional companies.
To-night (23rd Deo.) the Chilperic Amateurs will
endeavour to face the foe manfully instead of
warbling at their boots as on a recent occasion.
To-morrow the Associated Dramatic Clubs —the
very clotted cream of unchallenged talent—will
attack ‘ 1 Partners for Life ” and may Heaven have
mercy upon their souls. This is the proper time
for peace on earth, but some pieces are very hard
to digest.
** * *
The variety shows are likewise laying
themselves out to make the welkin ring. Prank
Clark has a liberal assemblage of legs at the
Gaiety, and the Victoria Hall minstrels are in
their 30th week of the ghost’s walk. At the
Exhibition and Masonic Hall there will be con-
certs of a dimly religious character on Christmas
Day, and the Oyclorama goes on rejoicing as
usual. Lastly, comes the Fasting Felix, of the
Waxworks, who grows more wan and interesting
to the ladies every hour. Many a fair creature will
breathe a sigh for Felix over her second plateful
of pudding, for the young man is unmarried, and
the little that is left of him looks yery nice. The
fact that his bowels (according to medical testi-
mony) collapsed long ago is peculiarly pathetic,
also the awful knowledge that two ounces of
corned beef would, under present circumstances,
entail his sudden death is calculated to make the
public heart bow down. Felix struggles along
satisfactorily in an arm-chair, all the same. He
has fasted for about 29 days already, and as a
slowly-diminishing sneer at the hog-like gorgeing
of humanity in general he may be considered
cheap at a shilling.
** * *
The waning light of “The Gondoliers”
having flickered out last Friday, an unusually
splendid (vide advt.) production of “Marjorie”
was to occur at Melbourne Princess’ the following
night. Miss Frances Saville and Henry Braey
infused new blood into the company, and
“ Marjorie” was doubtless a glittering success of
the first water. But further details are withheld,
for the reason that our budget had to be posted
to Sydney before the first performance took place,
so if it hasn’t taken place at all, friends will kindly
accept this intimation.
** * *
Grattan Riggs retains possession of the
Royal flure until Christmas Eve. He has
rollicked through his good old repertoire to fair
houses, generally supporting himself in fifteen
other characters, and now he is resting, as it were,
in “The Colleen Bawn.” At the Alexandra they
have ushered in the festive,season with “East
Lynne,” the slow dissolution of dear little Willie
and his mother being reckoned suitable to the
occasion. On Boxing Night, or earlier, the
indomitable Dampier intends to curdle much
young blood with “ The Phantom Ship,” in which
he plays the one and only poetic Dutch character
known to the English stage. Dampier should cer-
tainly make an effective picture of the eternal
Yanderdecken, whose blood-red bark is worse than
his bite, but if Miss Lily wishes to realise Thekla
she must buy a flaxen wig.
** * *
Other Boxing Night novelties are “ Dandy
Dick,” at the Bijou, “Chilperic,” at the Opera
House, and “ Aladdin,” at the Royal, with Jennie
Lee as the little hero. The temptation to remark
that one never sees a lad in the name-part is
irresistible. It is nothing much of a joke—only a
poor play upon words, anyway. But it used to
make our grandfather laugh.
** * *
There is a lack of amusements in Adelaide
just at present, the Cyclorama being the only live
show. Christmas time, however, will bring about
a change. The Theatre Royal, which has been
closed for alterations and repairs, will here-opened
by the St. Maur Company; Fred. Neebe has
arranged for the production of a pantomime—
“ Aladdin ” —at the Albert Hall; Wirth Brothers
introduce their Wild West Show on a big scale ;
while a number of other quiet amusements will
keep Adelaide well occupied during the festive
season.
** * *
The battle of “ The Messiah” is being
fought out in Maoriland. Wellington, in the
North Island, and Christchurch and Dunedin, in
the South, were all anxious to arrange with R. S.
Smythe for Madame Marian Burton to sing in
the Christmas performance of “The Messiah.”
The lady has accepted engagements from two of
these cities. After singing with the Motett
Society in Christchurch on Friday, 18th, she
sings on the Monday and Tuesday following at
two sacred concerts in the Wellington Opera
House with the Harmonic Society, of which
Encore Parker is conductor.
At Poverty Point.
The Melbourne and Sydney pantomimes, nowadays, can
only go the round of three or four subjects. In the
palmy days of pantomime look at the vast range of
nursery lore that used to be explored in London when
pantomime ruled the roast all round at Christmas. Blan-
chard, Nelson Lee, Byron, the Broughs, Talfourd, Buck-
stone, and other writers, were in full bloom, and Akhurst,
in Melbourne, held his own.
When Coppin had his row with Rev. Dr. Cairns, and
Cairns denounced him as “ a play-acting abomination,”
Coppin was the lessee of refreshment bars at Cremorne,
and put out a placard : “The Doctor has perhaps heard
of the Pharisee and the publican. Behold in me the poor
publican.”
Bouoicault’s second daughter, Mrs. Pitman, of New
York, has died since her father, childbed being fatal, but
the child is preserved.
The traditional acting lines seem to be getting obli-
terated. What has become of the Old Man, who used to
be indispensable in every piece, richly impersonated by
men like Farren and Chippendale in London and by
Rogers and Lambert in Australia? Then there is the
Charley Mathews and Billy Hoskins line—gone, while
even a droll like old Greville is put into an essentially sen-
timental part-Jaikes. The time will come round for him
to walk as the Ghost—again.

passenger to England by the Oratava.
In deference to the memory of Mrs. Booth, Fred Leslie
and Nellie Farren, throughout a recent brief run of the
“Ruy Bias” burlesque at the London Gaiety Theatre,
omitted their travesty of the Salvation Army.
A common newspaper-phrase runs—“ The amateurs
acquitted themselves very creditably.” The converse of
this is, we take it, “ The amateurs convicted themselves
most effectually.”
Editor Bulletin.— Sir, — l was always under the impres-
sion that the claqueur was an institution unknown outside
the continental theatres, but this illusion was dispelled at
a visit to a Sydney theatre the other evening. Somebody
at a little distance behind me applauded vociferously on
every possible opportunity (always starting the applause),
at times calling in a stentorian voice for “R !” Curious
to see the person who displayed such enthusiasm, I turned
round and saw—the man who takes the tickets!—Corn-
stalk.
From an Adelaide paper:—“Some one invited Lizzie
Hastings, of Garner’s Rooms, to sing at a concert in St.
Luke’s Schoolroom in aid of St. Luke’s Church, on Tuesday
night. Lizzie, with Bohemian good nature agreed. She
sang “Tit for Tat,” but that would not have mattered
had she not appeared in satin tights, with a satin cut-
away coat and a white bell-topper. Every holy eye in the
place that was not closed at once turned white, but the in-
cumbent, who presided, had to keep as Christian a coun-
tenance as possible. Lizzie sang two numbers in gay
style, knowing that she was giving her services and tights
in a good cause.

D’Orsay. In his imitation he set up a cabriolet and tiger
with the £3OO he received for “ London Assurance,” and
issued visiting-cards inscribed “Comte Dion de Bouci-
cault.”

Charles Wyndham was born in Liverpool, educated at
St. Andrews’, and in France and Germany, and studied
medicine at Trin. College, Dublin. He went through the
American Civil War as surgeon to the 19th Army Corps,
and became an actor in ’63, when he played Osrick at the
National Theatre, Washington, to the Hamlet of John
Wilkes Booth, Lincoln’s assassin.
Some years ago, says a writer in New York Truth,
Clement Scott wrote a column in London Daily Telegraph
concerning Sarah Bernhardt, in which he deftly applied
choice adjectives to her gown, and juggled with clever
similes to describe her passionate love-scenes, crowning
her in words as queen of her art. Bernhardt appreciated
this. She sent an usher with a note bidding him welcome
to her dressing-room. When he appeared at her door
(Scott is a handsome fellow), she seized him with both
hands and kissed him rapturously. When asked if he
liked it, Scott replied: “ I might have, but I had brought
a friend with me.”
Shrieked a French actress to her lover: “ I don’t like
men who are too much master of themselves.” “Nor I
women who are too much the mistresses of others,” re-
torted he.

young, still tipped with rare gold, still supplied with the
warm gurgle of the nursling, her plush-lined voice was

David Christie Murray is writing a comedy-drama
which will be called “ The Gilded Snob.” When that is
finished he starts on a play called “ The Barnacle,”
founded on records of convict-days obtained in Tasmania.
These plays, “Chums” and one named “False Fire,”
will be brought out in England. D.C.M. intends to write
only one more novel, describing the vicissitudes of a
.clever fool, and that will be entitled “ Paul Armstrong.”
He says he can only clear, at most, £2OOO a year by
literary work, while a* dramatic success would bring him
£20,000 in that time. He prefers the great uncertainty
to the small certainty.

a nightmare!

Edward Shuter, the great comedian, was a pot-boy in
his youth. A friend said to him once ; “ Why, Ned,
arerfit you ashamed to walk the streets with twenty holes
in your stockings Why don’t you get them mended?”
“ No, my friend,” said Shuter, “I am above it; and if you
have the pride of a gentleman, you will act like me, and
walk rather with twenty holes than have one darn.”
“ How do you make that out ? ” “ Why,” said the actor,
“ a hole is the accident of the day, but a darn is pre-
meditated poverty.”
Fred. Norton has opened a theatrical agency in
Sydney’s Victoria Arcade. He is a trier.
J. H. Clyndes, whose recent performances had made
him a popular favourite in Melbourne, sailed away for
Yurrup the other day. It was not until He had completed
his twelve months with the “Firm” that Clyndes began
to be affectionately spoken of by theatre-goers. The
actor grew on them, in fact, and was growing more than
ever when Messrs. W. and G. allowed him to go leaving a
visible gap behind him. Managers are not always quick
to hear the vox populi concerning individual artists, nor
apt to understand that a gallery-reception isn’t a safe
thing to swear by.

Walter Bentley, son of Dr. Begg, the great Scotch
disruptionist and Free Church divine, intends to visit
Australia shortly. Walter Begg—a schoolfellow, by the
way, of the present writer—was intended for the Church,
but, visiting New Zealand, adopted the theatrical profes-
sion, and made his debut at the Auckland Prince of
Wales. Since then he has acted as juvenile lead to
Ristori, Henry Irving and Mrs. Bateman, playing with
them such parts as Laertes, Malcolm, Lord Moray, Rob
Roy, &c., and being the Clarence of Irving’s Richard
HI. production, while his Alfred Evelyn, Claude Mel-
notte, Hamlet, Macbeth, Othello, Shylock and Romeo, in
a lengthy season with A. M’Neil at the Princess’ of his
native city, Edinburgh, were highly spoken of.
Melbourne musical world is as much disturbed as the
artists were by Gibson’s tinted statue of Venus. Here is
the trouble. At Christmas festival, in the Exhibition,
there is to be an angelic choir singing from the dome,
inside. The Maestros say it is Vandalic.

manager, left Sydney the other day for the Northern
districts of N.S.W. They did good business in Victoria.

The Picturesque Atlas people recently
netted in the Brisbane District Court, under their
cast-iron agreements, a large haul of recalcitrant
subscribers, who vainly put forward all the old
excuses—“lnever signed it,” “It wasn’t delivered
in time ” —and so on. It is useless to kick against
the pricks.
Mr. Bent was a witness in the Premier
Society case the other day, and Dr. Madden
started with the solemn question, “Are you
acquainted with the incident called the land-
boom ? ” Mr. Bent said that he was. As he lost
£750,000, and got involved in an accident, and
broke most of his legs and arms and features
during the boom, he probably recollected it.
An impression is slowly gaining ground
in the United Kingdom that the O’SheaEarnell
case was built upon “faked” evidence from first
to last. The affection which exists between Par-
nell and Mrs. O’Shea is unquestionable, but O’Shea
himself is a notoriously shady character, and more
than one paper now advances the theory that his
wife and Parnell simply let him “ fake ” to his
heart’s content, in order to get rid of the object
which stood between them.

EYE
LOTION
Tales of “ The System,”
No, XXIII.—The Platooning of Private
Trench.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
That February day on which E. T. Leppell,
Esquire, M.P. , had expressed (with a giggle) his
desire to witness the execution of a private of
H.M.’s 50th Regiment of Foot,* was drawing to
its close. The summer sun had changed from his
blinding fieriness to a burnished gold, and the
waters of Port Arthur responded to his with-
drawal by softening their amber to a pellucid
purple. Pools of • shadow clung round the Isle des
Morts; one solitary broad beam of crimson which
forced its way through tall sassafras, and taller
blue-gums decorated, ribbon-like, the bosom the
bay ; all else was sapphire or amethyst. The
zenith was veiled with a rosy film, and the clefts
and gullies of the eastern hills were filled with
flakes of vapour. Through the dusking green of
stately English oaks, the tower and pinnacles of
the church glistened with a white purity, and
even the old gaol, conceived though it was in the
best style of Governor Macquarie’s “demon
architect,” took upon its coarse commonplaceness
an unwonted aspect of graceful form and clear
colour. The anchorage contributed its peculiar
element of pioturesqueness to the scene in its tiny
fleet of craft. The Government vessel, the Lady
Franklin, H.M.’s 18-gun sloop, Favourite, and the
brig Tamar—the last flying her Blue Peter—lay
motionless as “painted ships upon a painted
ocean.” Earth and sea and sky wore so exquisite
a tapestry of beauty that even to the narrow
imagination of Mr. Leppell, M.P., the picture
that presented itself to his sight as he stood on
the elevation by Government Cottage, was
heavenly.
“ How very beautiful, to be sure !” he said to
Captain Boote, Settlement Commandant, “ and
how very kind of the Government to grant it to
prisoners when they might, for instance, have
made it a watering-place for the upper classes,,
like —like Brighton and Naples! Really, it’s my
idea of Paradise! I can’t understand anyone
being discontented here. ”
“ Yes, it’s really very pretty,” answered the
Commandant.
“ It only wants one thing to make it —ah, per-
fection !”
“And that is, sir?” Always open to effect
improvement was Captain Boote, and if the ex-
perienced judgment of the House of Commons
man suggested the addition of new and desirable
features to the scene (such as a pretty thing in
treadmills or a chastely-designed flogging-post),
why, Captain Boote would do his best to act upon
it.
“ Oh, music. Just think, Mr. Boote, supposing
this place was not a prison but a fashionable
resort for the upper-classes, how delightful a band
of music would be on such a night as this !” And
Mr. Leppell hummed a tune.
“Ah, yes!” The Captain’s assent was rather
qualified. There was no practical value in that
suggestion. “But you’ll have some music even
now, sir—that is, in a few minutes.”
Even as he spoke, the beat of muffled drum and
the sigh and sob of fifes stole on their ears from
the military cantonment. For Private Trench
had set out on his last march, to the sound of—
music.
# * *
From the battlemented tower a sergeant’s
guard had led the Condemned into the yard of
the military barracks. And there they struck off
his fetters. It was with a hand that shook
violently that the armourer’s mate drove out the
last rivet, for Private Ernest Trench was well-
liked in the regiment in the moments when he
was not very drunk. Then the devil in him used
to work havoc on the faces and the bodies of the
rank and file, and they did not love him quite so
well.
The men of the detachment —some seventy or
eighty —were gathered in groups waiting for the
“ fall-in ” bugle. Most of the convict-gangs had
been ordered in half-an-hour earlier than usual, sc
that their military watchers could be freed for
funeral duty. - The Civil side of the System on
such an occasion had not the least objection to
obliging the military by sacrificing some labour.
Nevertheless, though the Authorities had thus
done their best to confer eclat on the proceedings,
Private Trench, being in a discontented mood (as
it seemed to his officers), complained that suffi-
cient honour was not being done him. We know,
however, it was merely the cynical humour of the
scamp that made him say to Ensign Chick, who
was supervising the unriveting business :
“I should have had a full battalion at my
funeral if you had sent me to the city, sir, and a
thousand or two of the public. Here I’ve nobody
—only a company.”
“Oh, you’ll have the member of the House of
Commons as a spectator. He’ll make up for a
few companies and the mob,” Ensign Chick
replied.
“Good God! is he coming?” gasped Trench.
“God knows, I’ve done my best to keep him
away !”
“What the deuce do you mean, Trench?”
asked the Ensign’s astonished voice.
“Never mind—you’ll see, sir. But tell me
this, please, Mr. Chick —the member’s dau—the
ladies with him, they won’t come and see me
potted, will they ?”
“No, I should think not; it won’t be a sight
for ladies.”
“ Not if the ladies knew what they’re coming to
see. But it is not unlikely they’ll be asked to
witness a striking spectacle. You know, sir,
everything in this damned hole’s a‘spectacle’—
a spectacle for the gods, sir, to weep over.”
‘ ‘ Look here, Trench, don’t make insolent and
insubordinate remarks within half-an-hour of your
death, else— ”
“ Else what? You’ll report me, and give me a
flogging, as Police-Magistrate Stoneman did to
Convict Glancy—flogged him, and dragged him
from the triangles to the gallows? You’d flog
me, and then shoot me? Jove! I wish you
would ! That would be something for the M.P.
to report ! Come, Chickey—what a sweet little
chickey-mhuckey, he is, isn’t he? —try it on!”
And, leaning forward, he struck his officer with
his steel-locked hands. “There, chiek-a-biddy,
do your damnedst!” _ -
It has to be inferred that Private Ernest
Trench was not about to receive his death-wounds
in what Parson Ford would have called “ a com-
mendable spirit.”
* * *
The “ fall-in ” bugle blared out, followed
by the band-call. The officers went to their
stations. Chick, who was not a bad fellow at
heart, resolved to let the insult pass, and took 1 i i
post. Trench, from where he stood in the centre
of the yard, watched the young officer. “By
Jove, Sergeant, he isn’t going to report that blow, ’>
he said to the non-com. of the guard. “ I’ll have
to do something else .”
By which the Sergeant knew Private Trench
contemplated further devilry.
* # *
The ranks are formed, and now for
the procession. Presently the drummers and the
filers move to the head and the officers fall back
to the rear ; and now a half-company follows the
band, and another half-company is stationed just
in front of the officers. And there is a gap between
the two half-companies, and this is filled- —how ?
A hideous black thing moves forward, with an
erratic, centipede-like motion. It is Private
Trench’s coffin carried by six men. That fills
part of the gap.
Private Trench himself moves forward —smiling.
He fills something more of the gap—he, the hero
of the Occasion, takes up the least portion of room.
Much less than his coffin.
And then a section of twelve men interpose
themselves between the Condemned and the
second half-company. But before they do so they
receive and obey a command—reverse arms. They
alone reverse arms of the whole detachment. They
are the executioners.
And to the tap of drum and the whistle of the
fifes, the procession marches off in the slowest
time.
It moves into the lane from the barracks. As
he turns the gate-post Private Trench looks up to
the semaphore by Government cottage. As he
gazed, the ball, which signals to the chain-gang
to suspend labour, runs up to the peak.
“Bill,” Trench calls over his shoulder to the
soldier nearest to him in the firing-party—“ Bill,
it’s knock-off time for me. Hey-ho! What a
blessed sleep I’ll have to-night, and no reveille
and no morning gun ! Don’t you wish you were
me, Bill, lad ?”
» * #
Past the warders’ cottages with the white
palisades, past the Chaplain’s house with the
hawthorn hedge, past the Pier-road, went the
procession. And wondering-eyed children peeped
from verandahs and through fences, and damp-
A TIMELY RESCUE.
P OLICEMAN: “Throw yez a rope, is it? Well, then, begorra, here yez are!”
eyed women—one with a dancing baby in her
arms—crowded to catch a last glimpse of Private
Trench. For when he was sober, Erny Trench
was a favourite with the women and the children
of the Settlement. What pay the canteen did not
rob him of nearly always went for a child’s toy.
Past the hospital, with its magnificent pine-tree
clothed in the parasitic fragrance of the clematis;
past the churchyard, with its sentinel oaks —
“ Jack,” said Trench to the private on the oppo-
site side from Bill, “if you’re here in acorn time,
plant one or two over me, there’s a good chap ”
past the church, with its cruciform walls which
symbolised the Cross and satirised the System, to
the grassy square where the gibbet stood in
ancient Port Arthur days ; past the town to the
place of execution went the procession. And the
drums rolled, and the fifes wailed in the “Dead
March ” all the way.
Facing the wall which separated the square
from the garden of Government Cottage, the pro-
cession was halted.
Half-a-dozen words of command and as many
manoeuvres and then this is the disposition :
Under the wall, with its head a few feet from
the stones, the coffin.
Facing inwards, forming three sides of a square,
their fewness of number necessitating extended
order, the half-companies.
Ten paces from the line of men which confronts
the grim composure of the stone wall, the section
of executioners in double rank of six files.
To the right of the section, a group of officers,
and to its left, the drums and fifes, which beat
and sighed no longer.
And, in the centre of the square, the solitary
figure of Private Trench.
They (the officers) are apparently waiting for
some one.
“I thought,” growled the Captain Command-
ing, “that that Parliament man wanted to see
the show. I’ll give him three minutes’ grace.”
“ What’s that ?” said Dr. Slipshott, who, though
on the Civil side, had joined the group, as he was
acting as locum tenons for the military surgeon.
“That man, Leppell! He must not see the execu-
tion—there’ll be scandal otherwise.”
“What do you mean, sir,” sharply demanded
Captain Jourdain. “ There’s no scandal attached
to our administrative actions —we leave the
scandalous things to the Civil Establishment, ’
“Never mind what I mean, now, Jourdain
Don’t let us quarrel, for God’s sake! I meant
no harm —the scandal’ll be personal to the M.P.
He must not see Trench shot, or after, either.”
“ I fear no M.P.’s report,” persisted the military
man, still misunderstanding Slipshott’s meaning.
“J do everything decently and in order, and to
show you I don’t fear him or any other person, I
sha’n’t give the word till he does come, if I have
to send for him.”
“Well, if you must have my reason,” said
Slipshott, lowering his voice, “ take it! In con-
fidence, mind, for there’s a woman in the question.
All the Regiment believe Trench to be a ‘ swell ?’ ”
“ Yes ! ” broke in Jourdain and Chick.
“ Trench teas a ‘ swell.’ His real name’s Ernest
Trench Leppell—same.as this Parliament fellow’s
who is his uncle. Now do you understand ! ”
“Oh, the devil!” cried Jourdain. “Get it
over. Here, Provost-Marshal, the sentence! —
quick, man, quick ! Chick, see to the prisoner.”
* * *
Even now the scandal might have been
averted but for the appearance of the chain-gang.
The Provost-Marshal began hurriedly to declare
the finding of the Court :
“ That having most maturely, deliberately and very
attentively weighed and considered the whole of the
evidence and matter adduced in support of the prosecu-
tion together with what the prisoner has offered in his
defence, the Court is of opinion that the said Ernest
Trench is guilty of the crime laid to his charge which,
being in breach of the Articles of War, they therefore dp
sentence him, Private Ernest Trench, of the 50th Regi-
ment, to be shot to death at such time and place as ”
Thus far had the Provost-Marshal read when the
clank and draggle of irons, the laborious breath-
ing of overburdened men, and the squash of sqd-
dened boots, drowned his voice; The chain-
gang, returning “home” from their work in the
dock-yard, where they had spent the day in water
up to their necks, as was their custom, rested for
a moment on the rise by Gibbet Square ; and now
they stood, expressing their interest in the un-
wonted scene, by an excited jangling of chains.
The Provost-Marshal stopped at the clashing
tumult. He did not think his voice could over-
bear the maddening monotone of the chains. But
the noise sank more quickly than it arose. They
wished to hear, as well as others, did the chain-
gang.
“ Proceed, sir,” shouted Jourdain, stamping his
foot. “ What are you stopping for ? ” ■
But he was too late. Mr. Ernest Trench Lep-
pell, M.P., had arrived.
His own voice announced his arrival.
As he turned, in company with Commandant
Boote from the latter’s garden, his eyes had
caught the shameful spectacle of the chain-gang.
Fifty-odd wretches bound by twos to one long
cable, in muddy ,yellow-and-black, with fetters
and waist-chains, with “P.A.” and “Felon”
stamped so conspicuously on their clothing that
the, letters were distinguishable under the casing
of thin mud, might appal the sight of a man of
less refinement than a Tory member of Parlia-
ment. Add to the general dreadfulness of their
appearance the villainy indelibly printed on the
majority of faces, the gorilla-jowls, the soaked
garments, and the gluttonous hunger for excite-
ment which the military demonstration called into
their eyes, and it was no wonder that, in his
shocked surprise, Mr. Leppell made an exclama-
tion which reached nearly all ears and attracted
most eyes towards him.
“Great Heavens, Boote ! You don’t mean to
say these are men !”
If Boote answered, no one caught his reply.
For, from beneath the wall, from a spot which
lay flushed with the brilliant gleams of the fast-
fading sun, someone shouted the response: “As
much a man as you are, slave-driver Leppell!”
# * *
Private Trench, on his arrival at the
ground, had looked around, and not seeing his
uncle Leppell, had concluded that “the old
scoundrel ” would not be present, after all. And,
thus thinking, he thought he might as well die
quietly. There was no particular reason, he re-
flected, why he should kick up a fuss so long as his
relative was not present.
So, when Ensign Chick, at Captain Jourdain’s
order, moved forward with a couple of men, a
corporal and a ranker, and commanded Private
Trench to take up his position on his coffin, the
the Condemned obeyed.
“ I’m sorry I insulted you, sir,” he said to the
officer as they went towards the wall. “ The devil
was in me at what you said about—that Parlia-
ment man. I hate —Parliament, you know, sir.
It passed the Mutiny Act.” He laughed, and
Ensign Chick, now understanding, confusedly
said: “Oh, all right, Trench, say no more, but,
look y’ here —I know —well, I’m told you were—
once one of us—Halt I — Right-about face —I mean
—hadn’t you better think of — above, y’ know. On
your coffin, kneel I ”
“Above? God? Heaven? Bah? What I
think of is Hell! ”
Then they listened to the Provost-Marshal.
Whispered Chick : “ The handkerchief. Cor-
poral.' I '' And the Corporal was about to bandage
the eyes of the Condemned when Mr. Leppell’s
voice was heard in his strange, startled exclama-
tion.
“God ! That’s my uncle! ” the soldiers by the
coffin heard the Condemned say. “As much a
man as you are, Slavedriver Leppell! ” And every
man in Gibbet-square heard that last sentence and
saw the action which followed it.
As he uttered the words Trench sprang up
from the coffin-lid and wrenched himself from the
hold of the private. Then he rushed and leapt
across the space which separated him from his
kinsman. Between the intake of a deep breath
and its expiration he had covered the separating
distance, and though his wrists were gripped by the
sheeny steel-clips his hands went round the
honourable member’s throat.
# * *
It was but for a moment that the clutch
lasted, and yet the thumbs seemed to have done
their work on the windpipe. Leppell the uncle
gave one horrid gurgle—“ O God, Erny !” —before
his eyes protruded, and his face-flesh turned
ghastly. But a dozen hands tore Private Trench,
otherwise Leppell the nephew, away in time.
They would have hauled him back to his coffin.
But he said he would walk quietly. He’d done
all he wanted.
“You know, doctor,” he said to Surgeon Slip-
shott, “I’d rather this had not happened. But
there’s a Fate in it. He came out for some amuse-
ment and he’s got it.”
To Captain Jourdain he apologised for inter-
rupting the proceedings. “Sorry, sir, but that
man’s my uncle, and, of course, I was obliged
to pay my loving respects to him. ”
He stood upon his coffin before kneeling down
and gave a glance around.
The group by the prostrate form in the angle of
the square was separating and Dr. Slipshott was
dragging Leppell, M.P., to his feet.
“ Know why I called that man a slave-driver?”
the Condemned asked the Corporal holding the
bandage. “He’s a colliery-owner—works chil-
dren of six and seven years old in chains and iron
girdles—one of his boys, seven years old, had a,
strip of calloused flesh, five inches wide, on his
stomach. Great man, that man ! Defender of
British liberties, English gentleman, and so
forth. He’ll go home, and write a book—’ll say
Penal System admirable—military contented —
see if he doesn’t. But he won’t say he saw his
nephew shot!”
All this he said while the Provost-Marshal
droned out the rest of the sentence and its
approval by the Colonel-Commanding in Yan
Dieman’s Land.
And the Provost-Marshal, Ensign Chick fol-
lowed with his contribution to the rites.
“ Prisoner, on your coffin — kneel /”
“ Section, with ball cartridge—load.”
As the men bit their cartridges, Private Trench
knelt on the coffin-lid.
As they rammed home, and the Corporal, with
tremulous fingers, knotted the handkerchief over
the brilliant eyes, Private Trench said to the non-
com.: “That brig’s flying her Blue Peter —I’m
going out with her. And when I’m gone— ”
But Ensign Chick had ordered the front rank
to the “Present,” and the Corporal, pressing »
last nervous farewell on the doomed man’s
shoulder, had paced to the right, clear of the
bullets’ path.
# * *
There Avas a scuffle in the corner as the
front-rankers volleyed. Boote and Slipshott were
endeavouring to remove Leppell, M.P., from the
ground. But his white face glared oyer his
shoulder, and he saw his nephew spring up
madly, and fall, face downwards.
Whether it was due to the fading light, or to
prior excitement, the men, save one, had missed,
and the one bullet which had found a billet had
left every mortal part untouched. Private Trench
flung out his hands, stirred convulsively, tried to
raise himself, fell back, but breathed. Ensign
Chick’s further intervention was necessary.
“Front rank, left face, quick march. Rear
rank, quick march —forward.”
“ Halt—present — depress muskets .”
“For God’s sake, sir,” cried Private Trench,
“not in the back, sir—wounds in front, sir.”
“ Corporal,” was Ensign Chick’s strident utter-
ance, “ turn him over.”
“Corporal—when I’m gone—my ’kerchief—my
blood —give Slipshott for Miss—he knows.”
The rear-rank, with depressed muskets, fired.
And Private Trench lay supine, with his wounds
in front.

“ Canterbury ” Hutchison is the champion
draught-player of the N.S.W. Assembly, but he
doesn’t play for drinks.
Bancroft, the American historian, has
written 12 and 14 hours a day, and has averaged
eight hours for 20 years.

annual pension of £l2 to Mrs. W. Marsh in virtue
of being the oldest surviving colonist.
Brisbane Telegraph, an evening paper,
nays Editor Woolnough £lOO a year more than
Editor Rose receives from the Courier.
Robert Louis Stevenson says he hasn’t
much toleration for women, and thinks that this
may be because he doesn’t understand them.
During a recent visit to England Justice
Harding (Q.) rubbed noses with “Tummy,” and
was profoundly impressed with his affability.
The most successful press contributor ever
known in Brisbane was W. Lane, who earned £6OO
a year by casual work. It can’t be done now.

Bohemian.

poet’s widow.

Judge M‘Pariand.

to know why Queen Victoria doesn’t marry
the Pope. The only reason is that the Pope hasn’t
got enough money.

that it was Burton who discovered the source of
the Nile, and that he was the only Christian, bar
one, to enter Mecca.

On the eve of his departure for ’Ome, Sir
Henry Norman nearly lost the box containing
Central Queensland’s petition, signed by 7727 per-
sons. It got astray on a Sydney wharf and was
rescued by a boy whom his His Ex, rewarded with
a “bob!”
The Acting-Governor of N.S. Wales, aged
about ninety, is an active and unostentatious
gentleman. He clambers up a tree to see if the
tram is coming along. When he boards it he
shells out his penny tickets just like any other
youthful person.
Sculptor Boehm, R.A., (who executed that
fine lie in bronze, the Queen Victoria statue in
King-street, Sydney), has been found dead in his
studio by the Princess Louise. That was worth
dying for. All the same, no wise woman ever
found a dead man.

ton’s life, and a lot of his money, have been spent
in congratulating Tummy on being such a howling
success as Prince of Wales.

The life (by G. B. Barton) of William
Charles Wentworth, in three volumes, will be
published within the next 15 months —probably
by John Murray, of London. The work will be a
jnost attractive one to all Australians, and will
include original letters and anecdotes of fascina-
ting interest.
“De Leon’s Reminiscences,” a new book,
by an ex-American Consul-General, gives us a
fresh trifle about Thackeray, who told De Leon
that both he and Dickens were written out, pro-
ducing more dross than ore. Thackeray said he
was spinning out “ Philip ” because he wanted to
put a storey on his house at South Kensington.
The present Marquis of Ailesbury, the
sparkling youth who married Dolly Tester, the
high-kicker, ordered for his house a supply of
hand - grenades for- extinguishing fires. After
all the rooms were supplied there were six grenades
over. “What shall Ido with them, my lord ?”
asked the servant. “Better put them in my
coffin,” replied the Marquis.
A letter from the late Canon Liddon is
going the rounds of the press in which he
repudiates the allegation that once when preach-
ing before Mrs. Guelph in the Royal chapel he
imitated Bossuet and remarked, “ And you,
madame, must also die.” We should hope not,
indeed, and if Bossuet actually did use the words,
what an unmitigated snob he must have been !
A London literary paper had a glowing
notice of Princess Beatrice’s new book “ Memoirs
of Count Albert Erhbach,”or something like that,
but Labouchere cruelly applied a douche of cold
water, proper Aqua Pumpaginis, and “put on the
Maginnis,” by showing that the book hasn’t (or
hadn’t) come out yet! A beautiful sample of the
Koo-too. Sir Theodore Martin obligingly revises
Beatrice’s MSS.
Many years ago Jimmy Tyson, the mil-
lionaire, was employed by E. Morey, late P.M.,
at Townsville (Q.), at the rate of £1 a week and
rations. Morey was then a Downs’ squatter,
one of those who “came kerflop ” in the crisis of
the fifties. A few years since Tyson met his old
employer in Flinders-street (Townsville), and after
regarding him gravely for some seconds, solemnly
remarked: “Times is changed, Morey!” The
proposition was absolutely unchallengeable.
The Age lately said : “ The mere fact that
the Prince of Wales is not boycotted from public-
life in so moral a community as that of England
shows, of course, that his career has been abso-
lutely stainless, and we are entitled to hope that
his sons will grow up as admirable.” The fact
that about one person out of seventy will read the
foregoing seriously, sufficiently shows what the
public think of the Prince of Wales, who comes
of an historically immoral race, and has been the
constant companion of a most brilliantly immoral
set of rascals.
Mr. Edward Bulwer Lyttou Dickens (son
of the novelist), who is a N.S. Wales M.P., thus
lately discoursed on certain bribery charges :
He knew as much of Broken Hill as any member in the
House, and had there been any attempt at bribery it was
more than probable that he would have been approached
in the matter.
Which goes to prove that humour is not always
hereditary, and reminds us of the N.S.W. mejnber
of Parliament who, wishing to show how virtuous
he remained amid corrupt surroundings, ex-
claimed ; “ I assure you, Mr. Speaker I had not
been in the House six weeks before I had a hun-
dred bribes offered me.” And everybody appeared
to believe him.

AT THE ANTIPODES.
AUSTRALIAN ARTIST: “Well, how are you getting on?
EUROPEAN ARTIST: “I can’t get on at all, my work does not seem to take!”
AUSTRALIAN ARTIST: “Why don’t you teach?”
EUROPEAN ARTIST: “Well, if I can’t paint, how can I teach?”
AUSTRALIAN ARTIST: “It’s often done, you know; everything is reversed out here.”
POLITICAL POINTS
Griffith’s Northern tour is regarded in
N.Q. as a cautious crusade against Separation.
In Queensland 18,000,000 acres of Crown
lands have been, or are in process of being,
alienated.
When an attorney, who is an M.P., takes
a fee to see a private Bill through Parliament, he
should, as a matter of decency, be only permitted
to address the House from behind the bar.
The Australian Press almost unanimously,
in the present crisis, jumps on Parnell and
“ downs ” Home Rule. The exceptions will serve
to show the patriotic Irishman where his friends
are.
Judging from recent events, including the
cowardly throwing of lime into the eyes of Parnell,
the present Kilkenny election is being held in
order that the fable of the Kilkenny cats may
develope into a solid actuality.
The impression is that Speight has a card
up his sleeve, in case he should not agree with the
Victorian Government, and this is to form a
syndicate to take over the railways. Victoria, if
capable of considering such a proposal for a single
instant, must indeed have degenerated under the
Coalition.
Political influence has been banished'from
Victorian State railway management, but not
from the construction, so that the country is
logged up with a lot of political lines, run at a
loss. In one point, Speight is like Mirams-he
would have pulled through all right had the Boom
only held on.
Judge Harding, of Queensland, who has
just returned from ’Ome, observes that English
loyalty is much cooler now upon the appearance
of the Queen in public than he remembers it five
and-twenty years ago. Yes, Queen-worship is dying
by inches, and is doomed to become as extinct as
are the sacred cats of ancient Egypt.
Since his last expulsion, A. G. Taylor has
taken his seat in the N.S.W. Assembly on fully
two occasions, and Mr. Speaker looks like a man
who feels duly thankful. Probably both remember
the story of the much-married editor, who began
an article, “ How to make home happy,” and
whose wife wrote underneath, “Keep away from
it.”
“ The Times says that “the violent scene
at the recent meeting of the Irish party has justi-
fied the most sinister predictions as to what would
be the result of the establishment of an Irish
Legislature.” In other words, the Times is quite
sure that that the English are the only nation on
earth who are fit to govern either themselves or
other people.
Suppose a woman came forward and com-
plained to the law of an assault which had been
made upon her virtue six months before. Suppose
that at and since the time of the alleged assault
she had admittedly made no noise and no com-
plaint, what would now be thought of her story ?
Applying this supposition to N.S.W. politics,
what is the inevitable deduction?
To listen to the howling of the dingoes
one would think that 0. S. Parnell was the first
man who had got a little out of his course owing
to the allurements of woman, whereas history
proves that the Irish “ gentry ” to a man have
been notorious in all times for their promiscuous
and reckless attachments to women. The Lord
Leitrims, &c., however, did not agitate for free-
dom.
When a man gets sacked from Sydney
G.P.O, for drunkenness or theft, the Postmaster-
Gineral is not too hard on him, but very often
takes him back in a temporary position. In other
words, Minister O’Connor shows mild symptoms of
turning the N.S.W. Post-office into a reformatory
and inebriate-asylum. Up to date we notice that
the men who have been allowed to reform have had
plenty of political influence.
Can anything more forcibly illustrate the
need of social re-organisation than the fact, as
demonstrated by Government Statistician Hayter,
that out of a gross population of 854,434 souls in
Victoria (Anno 1881), no less than 280,957 persons
were engaged in domestic duties, i.e., non-produc-
tive and positively abject dependent occupations ?
This proportion equals 32'36 per cent of the total
community. What a state of civilisation !
Michael Davitt, writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, has made a hit with his
Labour World, though Heaven knows why, see-
ing that it is merely a second-rate Reynolds’ with
endless Irish hash, of which everybody is sick to
death. The Liberals are winning all the bye-
elections through the rank-and-file Unionists
“ coming on, ” and there’s not a shadow of doubt
that the elections will come soon—probably in the
spring.
Crick now asserts that when he made his
charges of corruption against Speaker Abbott and
Chairman {Melville he was mad-drunk. He also
claims that he resigned and was not expelled.
Now, as the corruption charges and the tendering
of his resignation were almost simultaneous, it
becomes plain that if he was not responsible for
the charges, neither was he responsible for his
resignation. Ergo , he did not resign but was
expelled. Q.E.D.
Subject for great historical picture; Mr,
Henry Hassall, M.P., selling at 12s. fid. a share a
thousand shares which he had bought at 17s. fid.
each, and thus losing £2so—all “in order that he
might stand in a thoroughly independent position
on the floor of the House. ” If Mr. Hassall knew
—and surely he must have known when buying—
that the Broken-hill Water Bill was to come
before Parliament, and that it was his duty to
vote on it, why did he voluntarily place himself in
an interested position by acquiring an interest in
the scheme ?

Society
We read that “there is considerable
speculation as to what part Lord Carington will
play in England—whether he will make an effort
in political life, or become again ‘ one of the Marl-
barough House set.’ ” Lord Carington will, The
Bulletin predicts, simply do that which is com-
mended to him by his glorified-stable-boy intellect
as the downiest thing to do.
# # *
Ex-Governor Carington has assisted at the
installation of that poor creature, Prince Albert
Victor of Wales, as Grand Master of the Berk-
shire Freemasons, on which occasion he remarked
that “the Freemasons of Australia will continue
loyal eternally. ” Eternity is a long time. Caring-
ton should have confined himself to remarking
that Australian Masonry might continue to
tolerate the Guelph brood so long as it hadn’t to
pay anything for supporting that miserable race
of epileptic nonentities—always provided that the
Guelphs aforesaid were not too obtrusively forced
upon its attention.
* * *
Mr. Midas Burdekin has been re-elected
Mayor of Sydney. In view of the disastrous
financial experiences of more than one open-
handed municipal magnate, no one else is “ game
for the Mayoralty while there looms ahead the
expensive prospect of having to civically instal
Governor Jersey. It is understood that Mr.
Burdekin will shortly depart on a visit to Europe,
and that he has a hazy idea of colliding with a
baronetcy or something during his absence from
the city in which he weekly reaps so rich a crop of
rents. Which reminds us that John Thomas
Smith, who was seven times Mayor of Melbourne,
was deputed during one of his terms of office to
present an address of congratulation to Queen
Victoria on the marriage of the Princess Royal;
and although he went to England with a private
secretary, and a chaplain, and had the honour of
kissing Her Majesty’s little finger, he returned
without a title, and accompanied only by the
secretary and chaplain aforesaid and a donkey,
the first quadruped of its kind imported to this
favoured land. It would be interesting to learn
what has become of this historic animal, which,
if still alive, should be conducted around all the
Australian public schools at the end of a string,
in company with the Soudan donkey, as a solemn
warning to the rising generation of the folly of
wasting your substance in a chase after false
glory.
* # #
A titled aristocrat bearing the name of
Galloway, is shortly expected as a guest at Go-
vernment House, Sydney. Apparently there is
an impression abroad that Galloway’s reputation
has been eaten away a good deal, for an English
paper apologises for him as follows ;
Lord Galloway has had the bad luck to fall into one or
two adventures of a very questionable kind, entirely
owing to the folly or mendacity of the other participators.
This is a case that, like Parson Gardiner’s, evi-
dently requires Christian sympathy, and The
Bulletin gets up on its hind legs to pray that a
great anathema may descend like tons of boiling
lead on these “other participators.” Compassion
would be thrown away on the crowd who led a
simple British peer astray, and annihilation is too
light a punishment. We want to see them live to
be old and toothless, gumming tough steak in a
cheap restaurant, where they slop the gravy on
the floor and keep a loose-jointed waiter who falls
down with the meat, and where there is always a
fly in the soup and a long hair in the butter. We
have read all about Lord Galloway. We don’t
want him in Australia. He is too unlucky.
# * #
A certain gallant British naval captain on
the Australian station, who was towing a fair and
goodly prize into the harbour of matrimony, has
just had his hawser cut.

Extraordinary announcement from the
N.Z. daily press
The Mayors of Christchurch, Dunedin, Auckland, and
Wellington have intimated to the N.Z. Premier their
approval of the suggestion that the Queen should be
asked to become godmother to Lord Onslow’s son.
Another suggestion on the subject, on which much stress
is laid, is the bestowal of a Maori name on the child.
Steward, please open the port-hole.

quially, in Maori, “Wait a Bit” —the idea being
to approximately render in Maori the name
“ Onslow.” This, we presume, is another link in
those silken bonds which, &c., &o.

Parkes’ private opinion on the Parnell divorce-
case, if the old man would kindly oblige.

Gem from N.S.W. provincial paper :
To the Editor Inveeell Times.
Sir, —We had a bit of a school treat a few days ago at
Little Plain ; and in course of talk one of my neighbours
said plants, trees, and so on, were male and female. That
was too much of a good thing for me, I can tell you. I
have set under clergy of all colours, college men too, but
none of them ever went so far as that. I think these new
fangled ideas do no good to the growing youngsters ; it
puts bad in their heads. And he was an old gray-headed
man that said it; but I put him in a corner by asking
him to show me a male grain of wheat from a female
e-rain. Will vou please tell me if it is true ’—Yours, &c.,
JOHN SLOOOME.
The poor schoolmaster runs against some big snags
in his time.
* * *
A man accused of a heinous offence at the
recent Sydney Criminal Sittings was discharged
after two trials, the jury on both occasions dis-
agreeing. On leaving the dock, the prisoner, who
had theretofore borne an unblemished character,
called God to witness his innocence, when Judge
Foster just as solemnly said he did not believe
him ! Perhaps so, but then Judge Foster said Mrs.
Louisa Collins’ case was one in whiohajury “might
reasonably disagree,” and yet, without a further
scrap of evidence being adduced, Mrs. Collins
was, as the result of another trial, handed over to
the hangman !

* * *
Albury hospital appears to be in a state of
squalor consequent on internal drunkenness. The
story is one of undispensed prescriptions, dirty
surgical instruments, and boozy officials. Several
witnesses deposed that the superintendent was
drunken and neglectful. The superintendent
himself alleged drunkenness against the matron ;
the cook said the superintendent was often drunk
and the matron sometimes so drunk that she
had to be put to bed. The night-nurse said
she had often seen the superintendent drunk, but
the matron “ only took a glass of ale which occa-
sionally went to her head slightly.” Eor which
sweet, merciful, musical, womanly phrase, The
Bulletin hereby returns its best thanks to the
night nurse.
* * *
Brisbane press is run purely in the
interests of the church, and ex-parsons collar all
the positions worth holding. The atmos-
phere of the Courier is Baptist; that of
the. Telegraph, Wesleyan ; and that of the
Australian, Roman Catholic. Rev. W. Roberts
does the bulk of the supplementary leader-writing
for the Courier, to the entire exclusion of the
professional scribes ; the ex-revs. Brentnall and
Woolnough do similar service for the Telegraph ;
while Father Fouhy and his priestly satellites
completely dominate the Australian. Another
“daily” is thus the daily prayer of the contri-
buting pressmen of the Northern city.
# * *
“Special Constable W.,” in a pathetic
appeal to the S.M. Herald, expresses the hope
that the N.S.W. special-constables will at least
be permitted to retain their batons as a memento
of the interesting epoch when they stood between
menacing unionism and cowardly “free” labour.
He further suggests that said batons may be
“handed down to generations to come,” and also
discloses the fact that he himself springs from a
race of heroes, his father having acted as “special ”
during the Chartist riots ! The Bulletin is
afraid “W.” gives a false initial. It has never
before seen Iscariot spelt with a “ W.”
# * *
A stewardess, who signs herself a “Victim
of the Strike,” tells a sad tale in a Sydney daily.
“ Many of my class,” she says, “who beforethe
strike lived entirely on board their vessels have, since
the strike, had to seek homes in boarding-houses.”
Daily she has called at the different shipping-
offices, but always to receive the same answer,
“No vacancy”—and this while her board-bill is
mounting daily and the landlady’s mouth is set-
tling down at the corners. Finally—
Last Thursday, after going to the different offices and
not obtaining a situation, I applied to a certain firm for
needlework and gained employment and worked from
Thursday till Saturday, making 180 buttonholes and sewed
on 78 pieces of tape, besides other trifles. I received the
large sum of 2s. 3d. for my work. Therefore it is not
possible to earn a living at needlework. I certainly think
that there should be a fund raised to assist us in sad
hours of need until we get employment at sea again.
The Bulletin sincerely sympathises with this
reduced stewardess, both in her inability to obtain
work at her accustomed occupation, and in her
change from cabin-table fare to boarding-house
provisions. But, as work is urgently wanted,
what is the mattqr with domestic service ? There
can be no pity for any able-bodied woman (and all
the stewardesses we have met have been particu-
larly able-bodied) who elects to work buttonhole
for her living while she can get at least 15s. a
week and board and every night out and a good
deal more in service, if she has the stuff of which
able-bodied ladies are ordinarily constructed. Nor
can there be any pity for any able-bodied woman
who openly says that she wants to loaf on a
“fund” until she can “go to sea again.”
# * *
The Ladies Dorothea and Estelle Hope,
sisters of the Earl of Hopetoun, have, as the
world knows, arrived at Melbourne on a visit to
their brother. The spectacle presented by the fair
pair mounted on donkeys at Port Said would not
have greatly impressed thejeunessedoreeoi Toorak.
Lord Hopetoun’s amiable sisters are remarkable
for their slim, not to say scraggy, figures. Each has
a lot of straw-coloured hair overhanging a pair of
apparently short-sighted pale-blue eyes, and a pale,
freckled face, from which projects a long, sharp
nose of inquisitive instincts. Altogether the
donkeys didn’t appear to be overburdened
either by the dignity or the weight of the ladies,
who drummed their heels against the animals with
as much energy and apparent enjoyment of the
exercise as ’Arriet on her bank holiday at ’Amp-
stead ’Eath. They did not join in the Britannia’s
dances during the voyage.

At Mackay (Q.), an Anglican curate has
been suspended by the rector and churchwardens
for attending a smoke-concert given by the local
Early Closing Association, What does Primate
Smith think of this ?
* # *
A distinguished surgeon who supports the
Medical Bill now before the N.S.W. Legislature,
and who does not wish to be thought illiberal,
proposes that, under certain conditions, reputable
non-diplomsed men who have practised con-
tinuously in Australia for 25 years—only 25 years,
mind you, not 125 years—be still allowed to
pursue their occupations. The grateful “quacks”
will probably present Dr. Tarrant with a gold-
framed sheep skin address.
#• # #
In reporting the libel-case, Bennett v.
Star, the latter paper quoted “ Ananias and
Sapphira” as “Annanias and Sophia.” The
E. News, by the way, used constantly to spell
“Ananias” with a double “n.” Great are the
complexities of the English language. If Ananias
has but one “n,”why should not “Bennett” be
spelt simply “Benet?” It would save a lot of
type and trouble.
# # *
Mr. W. E. Buchanan says that in the near
future the export from N.S.W, of frozen meat
will be scarcely second in importance to the wool-
trade. We are here tempted to enquire of Mr.
Buchanan his idea respecting the export of marble
men ?
# # *
At last meeting of the Austral Salon,
Melbourne, Mrs. Lupton read a paper on
“Humour” to a lot of other ladies. If Mrs.
Lupton herself knows anything about Humour
she stands unique among her sex.
# # *
There are 38 cases for hearing by the Vic-
torian divorce-court this month. Before the pre-
sent Act came into force there were less than that
number in a year, so things are 1200 per cent,
better than they used to be.
# * #
The first patient experimented on with
Koch’s consumption cure is dead, and several
others have since followed him. Possibly lie is
the exception that proves the rule, but when the
rule begins with an exception it excites unpleasant
comment. Perhaps it may turn out after all that
the only cure for consumption is a funeral, and the
funeral, unfortunately, is incurable.
* * #
Almost the only N.S.W. official who, after
having his name set down upon the Estimates for
an increase of salary, has been denied the advance
by Parliament, is the Sydney City Coroner. The
fact that Mr. Woore, a very old member of the
Civil Service, has made some mistakes since his
translation to town, in no way justifies the brutal
and cowardly attacks made on his personal
appearance under cover of privilege. However,
one thing is tolerably certain: It is hardly possible
to make a first-class coroner out of anything but
a hard-headed medical man.
* * #
An English beauty, asked the secret of
her rose-leaf skin, replied: “A virtuous
ancestry.” There you have it all in three words.
# * *
The Koek-Koek show of brand - new
pictures in Melbourne is good. Two, Holmberg’s
study of a Cardinal and two priests practising
music in the Vatican, and Kramer’s Judgment of
Paris, are priced at a thousand apiece. The
former is lusciously painted, the latter large and
taking. Venus has her clothes all off, Juno and
Minerva are slipping them on in disgust. Not a
picture of brilliant genius, but thoroughly work-
manlike. Who is to buy it? No public gallery—
but we forgot, Dale Chloe still refuses to blush in
Adelaide.
# X #
This hideous Johnston tragedy at Ballarat,
in which a mad broker slays his four pretty
children and mortally injures his wife, brings to
mind two most piteous affairs in Melbourne - most
piteous because the hand of Nemesis was in no
way traceable in them. In Walsh-street, South
Yarra, a few years ago, a rich man’s luxurious
two-storied wooden house was burnt to the
ground, with all his children (four) in it, his wife
being dragged out insensible ; the father was away
on business up country. In North Melbourne, a
man and his wife locked up their wooden cottage,
leaving their three children asleep in bed, and
went off to see a funny play at the theatre, but on
their return the house was burnt, and all the
children too. The one bright feature of the
Johnston horror is that the wife, after lingering
awhile, died, instead of living only to realise-that
all her children had been slaughtered by the
maniac her husband.
* * #
Dr. Gresswell, of Melbourne, tells people
not to kiss the dead. Quite right. Kissing the
quick provides quite enough trouble for the
average man.

combat cl outrance, and the other, a well-known
and gifted medico, said that that would be unpro-
fessional, and so the affair lapsed. Now the repre-
sentative of democracy asserts that the aristocrats
are all cowards and that the possession of thousands
a year renders life more valuable than honour,

Hundreds of cricketers, and the sporting
world, attended Midwinter’s funeral; but at the
funeral of Esmond, the Victorian gold-discoverer
and Eureka Stockade hero, there were scarce
enough to carry the coffin.
# # #
English bank-directors are now forbidding
their clerks to marry on salaries under £l5O a year
—at least, they tell them to go elsewhere, if they
do. It is a fact that in several of the old estab-
lished banks before the Crimean war, notices were
hungup to the effect that “the employes would
please not wear moustaches during office-hours.”
The rule still prevails in one or two offices.

on the N.S.W. Postmaster-General’s Department,
we learn that the gorgeous individual who patrols
the portico of the G.P.O. and makes himself so
agreeable, is 56 years of age, and receives a
“ stipend ”of 7s. a day. His resplendent scai let
uniform is provided by the Government, but he
has to find his own glittering display of medals,
* * *
Some of the ladies connected with the
N.S.W. Post and Telegraph Department are much
annoyed at the shameful, horrid, mean publicity
given to their ages in the recent Royal Commis-
sioner’s report. The majority of the fair creatures
are over 30 years; one “ Miss ” is 40 years, another
17, and another comes up to 55 !

At length Chief Justice Lilley’s prayer is
to be granted. In Queensland a Royal Commis-

* * #
Twenty-seven sea captains permanently
reside at Thursday Island (Q.) The visitor to
that secluded haven who desires to be on the safe
side will address every man he meets as “Can-
ting !”
# * #
One of the passengers from Brisbane for
London by the ill-fated Quetta was an old chap
who used to sell ice-cream in Queen-street. His
“ portable property ” on his last voyage included
some 700 sovereigns, all, as the Chinaman would
say, “ with the horsee head on one si’!” They
are among the “relics” which the Arab and
Malayan divers haven’t yet recovered.

That bottle of bank-notes found in
‘' Thunderbolt’s ” cave knocks the stuffing out of
the story that the bushranger is still alive and a
prosperous farmer in N.S. .Wales. Walker shot
Thunderbolt as dead as a door-nail.
* # *
A really good day’s work was done in
Melbourne one day last week, when three corpses
were fished out of the Yarra within four hours.
Two of them were “ dead-broke,” but the other had
foolishly neglected to drink the sum of 2s. 10d.,
which will now be absorbed by a paternal Govern-
ment.

In the (N.Z.) divorce-case of one Atkinson,
last month, the circumstantial evidence was the
governess’s back-hair comb found in Benedict’s

# * #
The Melbourne Quakers still keep it up
at their meeting-house in Russell-street, a very
old-fashioned establishment, about 35 years of age.
One Sayce, a good old English trencher-hat, has
been drawing some attention there of late with his
lectures, but Quakerism seems pretty well wiped
out, and they become as other men are—or even
this publican.
« * *
Last week, at Manly Beach, that interest-
ing old gentleman, the Rev. D. P. M. Hulbert,
was burnt out of his cottage and lost almost all
his little worldly possessions. There is now a
movement on foot to place Mr. Hulbert and his
wife—both nearing their 80th year—in as good a
position as that which they occupied before their
misfortune.
* * *
Messrs. H. S. Hop wood and W. Heron,
two rising artists, who are departing for Paris to
study, were given a send-off at Paris House,
Sydney, last week by some Sydney confreres.
The Bulletin hereby requests Messrs. Hopwood
and Heron to be so good as to enquire and report
whether it is true, as alleged by ’Awken, M.P.,
that the French Salon gets its ideas on the subject
of realistic art from the Sydney Post-office
carvings ! If Paris merely copies Sydney, it will be
merely waste of money for anyone to seek artistic
instruction in Lutetia.
* # #
A Cooktown (Q.) fair one entered a Chinese
store the other evening to make some purchases.
Then she picked up a pair of white satin slippers,
and observed that she wouldn’t mind taking
them as a present. “All li,” characteristically
and naturally said the slimy Tartar, “yougivee
one kliss allee samee Englishman!” The fairy
fled, but the Chow pursued and overtook her.
Then a white hero, “ George the Bullocky”
stepped up and dealt the Flowerylander a biff on
the ear which has confined him to the local
hospital ever since. Thus was virtue championed
and wickedness put down with a strong hand.
# * *
“WELLY LI !”
Editor Bulletin. Sir, I read your
article on “ Stuffing ” with much interest, as from
what I know of Chinese cooks the less we have to
do with them the better. I have heard much
criticism on the subject; some averring that
“Stuffing” was merely founded on the writer’s
fancy. However, “The Bunyip’s ” experience
happens to tally with my own, and if what I now
write should be the means of deterring even one
person from retaining the services of a Chinese
cook, I shall feel rewarded. Some years ago my
husband and I were staying at one of the best
hotels in Brisbane. We had then just been
married, and as my husband was rather a gourmet
I took particular note of the dishes he most pre-
ferred, with a view to the time when we should settle
down in our new home. My husband particularly
praised the hotel pastry, which certainly was very
light and flaky, and in the after-time I never
could quite succeed in pleasing him in that direc-
tion; for however much I excelled myself, he
always wound up by remarking, “My dear, I
wish you could make pastry like that cook at the
Tip Top”!! It was most exasperating to find
myself compared unfavourably with a Chinaman ;
and when next he visited Brisbane we went, as
before, to the Tip Top, whose landlady, after some
demur, gave me an introduction to Ah Man, the
cook. Insinuatingly holding a half-sovereign ’twixt
The State-school refreshment hour. Chewing-gum, one halfpenny a stick.
finger and thumb, I politely asked the yellow man
a few leading questions. “ Did he use baking-
powder or anything like that ?’’ “No ; only flour,
suet, water, white of egg.” “ Did he mix it in a
particular fashion?” “No; allee same way.”
And that was all I could extract for my half-
sovereign. While on the hotel roof one afternoon,
armed with a strong pair of opera-glasses, I by
accident discovered that I could see right down
into the kitchen —a wooden erection to the rear of
the main building, lighted by a large skylight,
now, to my joy, wide open. Looking through the
gap, I could see the cook rolling out a mass of
dough on the table. Then I could see him beat
up the white of an egg or two and put it in the
cup on the table beside him. Here was the crux
of the position, and I nearly let fall the opera-
glasses in my trembling anxiety. He then took a
sip of the contents of the cup, and—oh, horror !
spat it through his broken, blackened teeth all
over the rolled-out mass of dough. I wonder if
that was what made his pastry so “ welly li’?” —
C. Bun.
# # #
A number of Melbourne ladies—mostly
married, by the way—have lately taken to heaving
their idolatry at a certain comic-opera singer, who
is alleged to regard his worshippers with a look of
horror similar to that which Joseph wore when
Mrs. Potiphar was shrieking her soft nothings
after the young Jewish gentleman’s retreating
form. The englamoured females, rendered de-
sperate by the chortler’s icy glare, have given
bold advertisement to their devotion by throwing
flowers across his stage track, and pelting him with
lace-edged fans and smelling-bottles, and things,
all of which the unresponding cherub tramples
under his flat feet or contemptuously kicks into the
orchestra for fiddlers to scoff at. Occasionally, so
rumour says, a disgusted husband swoops down
upon his lawful lump of disappointed loveliness
and packs it off to Europe by the next
boat, Europe being the place where we
send our impressionable matrons in exchange
for the male parties who mash ’em. Any w r ay, it
is evident that the tuneful Joseph heretofore
referred to has been an unwilling cause of much
tender idiocy and social disquietude. And as
woman’s love is too beautiful a thing to lie
wasted on the desert air, and no daughter of Eve
should stand in want of a warning voice when
she feels anxious to make an object of
herself, The Bulletin now takes oath and
testifies that this worship of Joseph is founded
upon a general misunderstanding of the real facts
of the case. In the first place, he isn’t half
enchanting enough to die for, either as an artist or
as an ordinary everyday sort of human being.
He lacks the all-important qualification of a
wicked eye, and appears to be quite hopelessly
respectable. Joseph, in fact, is just a pleasant,
wholesome, good-tempered looking fellow, owning
one wife and a liberal allowance of children. He
has done absolutely nothing to justify his sudden
elevation to the pinnacle of perfection, and he
doesn’t like the pinnacle now he is perched upon
it. Therefore, we exhort the maidens of Mel-
bourne to set their minds upon something tangible,
and beseech the demented wives and mothers to
rest content with their faithful Potiphars, who
might, in many instances, look quite equal to the
other fellow if they were limelit and faked up.

“The Queen was much distressed when
she heard that the Countess of Jersey had been
seized with typhoid fever,” says a London
twaddler. You bet! Our Jersey lily had visited
the Queen only a few days before, and Her
Gracious naturally fell a-wondering how many
germs had been left on the royal premises.
# * *
Gaslight robbery goes on here, there and
everywhere. We read that the Parisian Gas
Company, “a bloated monopoly,” has been
frightened by the electric light into making a
reduction in prices and returning a small per-
centage of profits to consumers. How long before
Australian consumers will insist upon getting
back a little of their own ?
* # *
Lovely woman is evidently supposed to
take her Xmas literature sadly. Of three short
stories in the Lady’s Pictorial, the first leaves
the heroine with her head upon the breast of her
dead second lover, who had murdered her first
lover; the next finishes with the heroine looking
out of the window for the gallant boy who threw
himself upon some quicksands to drown; and the
third maudlin narrative reaches a climax in the
heroine’s watery grave. All seems to be cold,
blank misery, with the exception of a few
millinery advertisements.
THE CALF-AND-BRE ASTBONE BRIGADE.
Editor Bulletin. Sir, Sydney Echo
offers £5 ss. for a “ Boating Song.” My friends
say that lam not likely to get those guineas. So
I send the effort to you :
Blear-eyed and stale “ drunks,”
A boat-load of city skunks,
Foul-mouthed and rude,
And more than half-nude,
On “Our Beautiful Harbour.”
A very small cap
On a thumping big head,
With knees wide apart
And feet at full spread
On “Our Beautiful Harbour.”
Bare-legged and bow-legged as well,
Feet raw, sun-burnt and wet,
Toe-nails as big as an oyster-shell
All the same, encrusted with jet,
On “ Our Beautiful Harbour.”
Arms stuck akimbo,
Brain in a nimbo
Bemuddled with beer;
Hair cut to the scalp by a “ sixpenny ” barber,
Blinking and winking
We take a place at each pier,
Jostle and grin at the girls with a leer
When they’re out for a day on the harbour—
On “Our Beautiful Harbour.”
S. CRIBE.

Russell Sage is quoted as saying : “ I never
lent a dollar which was returned to me unless
legally secured. I niver backed a note I didn’t
have to pay, and I never recommended a man for
a situation who didn’t turn out to be as mean as
he could. I now let other men run the philan-
thropy business. ”
It was stated, the other day, at a meeting
of the Melbourne Evening Standard share-
holders, that the employes voluntarily submitted
to a reduction in their wages of 10 per cent, rather
than see the size of the paper reduced. This
absurd eight-page notion is the rock on which so
many journalistic ships have split. The Age won
its spurs and achieved its greatest victories and
politically beat the Argus as a big four-page
paper, and a big four-page outspoken, honest
mornieg sheet, Radical to the back-bone, and run
by another Ebenezer Syme, would make a fortune
either in Melbourne or in Sydney. To make a
daily pay, its size must be proportioned to its
advertisements. A single column of advertise-
ments in a daily paper should (20 inches at 4s. per
inch nett) mean £4 daily or £24 weekly, which is
£1250 a year. Thus, two columns of advts. per
day pay interest on a capital of £50,000 at 5 per
cent. Supposing, however, that a daily has two
columns of advts. less, then it has to provide two
additional columns of matter and type-setting,
which latter item alone means, say, 28s. per day,
£8 Bs. per week, or £420 per annum—the interest
on £B4OO at 5 per cent. In clutching at this false
glory of eight instead of four, fortunes have been
thrown away in spinning out cheap advertisements
on expensive paper.
A Perilous State.
The moment a child is born; that moment he
begins to die. Therefore beware of disease making
inroads into the system. Probably one of the
earliest signs is slight dyspepsia which cannot be
attributed to errors in diet, want of fresh air, &c.
It is a nervous kind of atonic dyspepsia, some-
times combined with acidity from liver sympathy.
There is not the same relish for food, and so relief
from this state is sought for in piquant relishes,
wine, or tea. Another symptom is an uneasy or
tired feeling about the head, fulness it may be, or
sleeplessness and stupidity, especially after eating.
The brow is often hot, so is the top of the head,
and the hand is pressed wearily across the eyes.
Life begins to lose its brightness, then things
begin to go a trifle wrong, and there are moments
of peevishness and irritability. Lowness of spirits
is succeeded by want of sleep. There is danger
ahead, if not, indeed, close at hand, and happy is
he who sees the signal in time. Recourse should
immediately be had to some genuine tonic restora-
tive, which will restore the consumed nervous
tissue, and most probably Clements Tonic is the
best for this purpose, it having had uniformity
good results every time we have seen it used. In
cases of debility, nervous prostration, bilious and
nervous headaches, anaemia and all diseases arising
from poorness of blood and indigestion, Clements
Tonic can be used with confidence. It is not a
quack remedy, and unlike other patent medicines
its proprietors do not claim that it “will cure
everything ” from lock-jaw to bad spelling. But
for cases of affections of the nervous and digestive
systems it is specially serviceable. E. C. Gutt-
ridge, Esq. (Messrs. Mauri Bros.), writes 486
Kent-street, Sydney. Dear Sir,— It is only justice
that I should write to you of the benefit I have
derived from the use of Clements Tonic. Two
years ago I was brought to death’s door by typhoid,
and from that time until recently I was quite
destitute of strength and energy, my daily occu-
pation was severe toil, and I had on several
occasions to take complete rest from business on
account of sheer weakness. Eventually I com-
menced to take Clements Tonic, and with the
first bottle I commenced to regain strength, and
you can readily understand that I continued its
use, and now have taken six large bottles. My
strength is completely restored ! I give all the
credit to Clements Tonic, and having obtained so
much benefit from its use I think the least I can
do is to induce others to use it. W. J. L. Kyle,
Esq., Postmaster, Manilla, N.S.W. writes, July
23rd, 1889. Dear Sir,—At the beginning of the
present winter I suffered for several weeks from
very severe attacks of neuralgia and toothache.
After trying several remedies (?) without any
relief, I heard of a friend who had derived great
benefit from Clements Tonic, and believing that
my system wanted bracing up, I sent for a bottle,
and before I had finished it the neuralgia had
quite left me. At first I was sceptical as to the
merits of Clements Tonic, but am now fully con-
vinced that it does all you claim for it, and you
can send me another bottle, as a stand-by against
all future attacks.

Brisbane gaol-warders are on duty for an
average of fourteen hours a-day during 665 days
a-year, while their pay is much below the dead-
level of labourers’ wages.
A Melbourne paper lately wrote that
“ the Blessedness of Poverty ” was all humbug,
whereupon Archbishop Carr (R.C.) took up a
lance for Poverty, with an oration to the effect
that it was the very best thing. The paper
returns to the charge with an assertion that Arch-
bishop Carr gets £6000 per annum, and Cardinal
Moran £9000.
Under the title, “A Shepherd and hi 8
Flock,” we read the following in a Broken Hill
paper : —“ t The Rev. A. J. Birt pleaded guilty to a
charge of allowing his goat to stray in public
places. He stated that the goat came into his
yard some time ago. He tried to find the owner,
but could not. As soon as he received the
summons he sold the animal. Defendant was
fined 5s and costs, £2 8s 4d in all.” Which makes
us smile, but we don’t know why.
One of the best things that G. H. Reid has
got off for a long time was said the other night in
tjie N.S.W. Legislative Assembly. Lakeman,
M.L. A., was bemoaning the fact that he could not
get any attention paid to the rabbit-question,
“Why don’t you include female suffrage in a
rabbit-bill ? ” said Reid, looking first at Lakeman
and then at Premier Parkes. “ It would then go
through flying. ”
The Contractors’ Lien Bill has successfully
emerged from its third reading by the Victorian
Lower House, despite the agonising squeals of the
Melbourne Argos and the quondam book-censor
Patterson. The measure was introduced to enable
contractors to consider the buildings which they
had erected at their own expense as their own
property until the proprietor {sic ?) had paid for
them. It will be a decided feather in the cap of
the Labour party should the Bill survive the class
opposition with which it will be met in the Upper
House, as the amount of wages lost to builders’
employes through premeditated insolvencies on
the part of the lords of the soil has been computed
to total no less a sum than £17,600 annually.
