The Referee
Mr. “Jack” Want’s endeavour to alter the
method of electing the A.J.D. Committee is a laudable
one, but as it 1s opposed by the Union Club, that settles
the matter. The present mode of electing the A.J.C.
Committee differs altogether from that adopted by all
other known clubs, and is this: The A.J.C. Secretary
sends to e|cji member, wherever located, a ballot-paper
hearing the names of the candidates ; simultaneously the
\\tt\e'inipm%im in imperio which sits on A.J.C. matters
at the Unrori Club-house, Sydney, circularises the mem-
bers asking them to be sure and send down their ballot-
papers as usual to the Union Club, doncherknow, and the
members-of the irnperium aforesaid, who know' what is
the right thing to do, yersee, will save them the trouble
of voting. (The rule of the A.J..C. at present is, that a
member, w'hen voting by post, shall, after striking out on
the ballot-paper the name of the person or persons for
whom he does not intend to vote, sign his name inside
the envelope in which he returns the ballot-paper, other-
wise the vote will be informal.) The plan now adopted by
the country member w-hose delight it is to strengthen
the hands of the A. J.C.’s conservative tow r n-ring is not to
mark the ballot-paper at all, but simply, after writing his
name inside an envelope, in accordance with the regula-
tion aforesaid, to re-post his ballot-paper in a state of
virgin purity to the Union Club irnperium, who, after
they have carefully struck out the names of their enemies,
hand it in to the A.J.C. And thus it happens that at a
general meeting you will find some member of the Union
Club-sailing in with some seventy or eighty signed returned
envelopes, containing ballot-papers, in his hand and
plumping them down en bloc. This is part of the machinery
by which the A.J.C. is made so “ select.” It is likewise a
part of the means by which the A.J.C., as compared with
the V.R.C., is such a dismal failure and so out of touch
with the crowd.
* * *
In the championship seulling-match, there is
not much doubt M'Lean had Kemp at his mercy from
start to finish. The alleged foul could in no case have
made any difference to the result of the race. Mr. M’Lean
is now full-fledged champion of the world, and thus is
fulfilled the prediction of E. Hanlan, Esq. Kemp at first
took his gruel badly, saying he would never again row a
race umpired by Mr. Blackman ; that he did not try to
win after the foul, and that he did not consider the win
put M‘Lean in the first rank of scullers. Which may be
quite true, seeing that somehow or other no one ever
seriously regarded Kemp himself as being in the first rank
of scullers.
*# . *
The Spencer Brothers, dentists, of Sydney, are
probably unrivalled in the w r orld in their knowledge of
the science of rigging a man up in a boat. M‘Lean,
according to all accounts, could not have been better
fitted-up in the championship-race if his boat and sculls
had been intended to chew something.
# # #
M‘Lean is as yet devoid of one accomplishment
—the art of oratory. Tub Bulletin cautions him to
abstain from learning. By the time an oarsman has learnt
to spout, he has generally forgotten how' to row.
* # #
Salvator, a son of Prince Charlie, has got over a
mile in Imin. 35Jseo. on the Coney Island track. Boolka,
at Flemington, and Kingflsh, at the Hawkesbury, hold (if
we remember rightly) the Australian record with Imin.
40sec.
# * *
Talking of fast-mile times, the bicycle record is
Rowe’s (U.S.), 2.29 4-5 ; the tricycle record, Lee’s (Eng.),
in 2.33}; the fastest mile on ice-skates, M’Cormack
(Canada), 2.58; footracing, by George (Eng.), 4.12};
trotting in harness, Maud S., 2.8 j.
* * , *
Does it not seem a strange thing that the old-
timers did not find out that a man had “ a point ” on his
chin? Read Boxiana from the time of Figg to Sayers,
and see how few fair “ knocks-out” were given. Allowing
that the old-timers did not “ try for thepoint"’ sometimes
surely in their hundred-round batterings they got on to
the spot by accident? It looks much as if a blow from a
glove has a more deadening effect than that from the
naked mauley.
# # #
The subject of the old bruisers brings to mind
the fact that Joseph Choynski, Esq., of America, now' in
Sydney, is complaining of the meanness of an Athletic
Club in offering only £l5O for a “purse” for the proposed
meeting between this noble bruiser and Mr. Owen Sullivan.
Shades of the old-time gladiators ! Only £l5O for a
meeting with pillows between a man who was beaten in
America by another beaten man and a big rough-and-
ready fighter from the back blocks who has not yet made
his debut on metropolitan boards !

One of the eleven “lady-cricketers” of Mel-
bourne has written thus to a local journal:—“ Sir, —I see,
again, in your paper of Dec 4th, about the Lady
Cricketers. I think you had better make sure. Before
you are called upon to prove all those false reports about
Drink, and can you make sure you have not made a Mis-
take in the House Where jugs, and Bottles, go in and
out. and keep a Brewery, on The premises. Sir I wait
an early apologue I remain a Lady cricketer and not a
Beer drinker. P.S. Sir. I cosider I have been greatly
insulted.”
• # *
It is related of Midwinter, the recently-deceased
Australian professional cricketer, that in England one day,
after a match, a well-known English amateur, who has
been in Australia, said, “ Carry my bag over to the
station, ‘Mid.,’ and I’ll give you a shilling.” “Mid.”
simply answered, “Carry mine over, Mr. , and I’ll
give you two shillings.” This story has been told before—
of Emmett, the English professional.

evidently a lot of big game in the Far North.

Borneo, in September last, a fine bull elephant was shot

# # #
Scotch College beat Wesley College by one
innings and 52 runs for the Melbourne Public-schools
championship, last week. Donahoo, on Wesley side,
proved himself the coming Bruce, both in batting and
bowling. He is bound straight for the Victorian Eleven,
and the Australian, too.
# * *
Humphrey Oxenham laid the Pyrmont Punter
600 to 100 ‘each of two’ in the Fairfield Handicap, and as
they ran first and second the backer had a good wager.
* * *
A starter of bipeds is not necessarily a good
starter of quadrupeds, as evidenced by the mulling at
Lillie Bridge night after night. Half-an-hour to start
four ponies is rather too much of a good thing.
* # #
The war between the N.S.W. Pony Association
and the Sydney Driving Park Club appears to be nearing
a termination. The Association has removed all dis-
qualifications, and declares that from Monday week last
any pony or jockey performing at an unregistered meeting
will be for ever debarred from racing on any of the
registered courses. It will be interesting to note how
this will affect the Driving Park, which up to now has
been able to hold its own.
* * *
George Parr, the famous Notts, cricketer —a
perfect misogynist, as well as misogamist—has got married
in his 65th year. George wants a nurse—he has been
seriously ill—and now his housekeeper becomes his wife.
Cricketers used to say that the only kiss ever bestowed on
Parr was by the tatooed Queen of the Maoris during an
Australasian tour.
# * #
Joe Thompson laid and lost Sydney Fry, a son
of the great English bookmaker, £5OOO to £35 about
Sheen and Alicante for the Cesarewitch and Cambridge-
shire.
* # #
It is said that a prize of £3OOO will be offered
for any trotter that can cover a mile in 2min. on the
straight track at Monmouth Park, New York. The trial
is to come off next September, and the probable triers
will be Maud S., Sunol, Axtell, Belle Hamlin, Guy, and
Palo Alto.
* * #
So far, the junior cricketers who have been tried
in their maiden efforts have not shown that pluck for
which Australian cricketers are famous. When they face
such men as Turner, Callaway, and Garrett their hearts
take residence in their boots, and after a failure or two
they drift back into junior clubs.
* # *
The Australian Athletic Club gave the first of
their Saturday evening shows on the 13th inst., but the
public, which loves blood, will not “ catch on ” to mere
“ scientific ” tapping matches, and as a consequence the
attendance was small. The most noticeable set-to was
between Mr. George Seale and the American, Joe Choyn-
ski. This was Mr. Seale’s first appearance after a very
long retirement from the boards.
* * *•
The loss of “ Sammy ” Jones is a serious one to
the Sydneyites. Sammy was one of our best performers
in intercolonial matches. A match was never over until
he was disposed of.
* # *
Mr. “Jack” Want pulled off the double at
Warwick Farm with his horse Grayling. As usual; when
he thinks he has a good thing, Mr. Want piled it on
properly.

# # #
Prior to leaving for America, Jem Hall
endeavoured to put through Starlight, at the Apollo
Hall (Melb.), in eight rounds. He knocked the musky,
dusky one down four times in the fourth round and five
times in the fifth, when Starlight cried a go. If Hall had
only had a little more devil in him, and could follow up
an advantage better, lie would be a world-beater.

All who have had anything to do with the
Militia of Victoria will regret the death of that fine’fellow
Colley, the armourer, who doctored his last Martini-
Henry a week or two ago. He was the most familiar
figure at the Rifle Butts, and was known to all the Inter-
colonial visiting teams.
* * *
Lizzie Nelson, a lady of something under Bst.,
is anxious to box any feather-weight angel in Australia
for a£s trophy—Marquis of Queensberry rules. Regular
business in the sweet girl pugilist line is almost a dead
letter, but the writer has an imported maid-of-all-work
who flatly declines to take a week’s wages and go, so if
Lizzie will kindly knock her out as soon as possible she
can take a trophy of the value above stated. Further-
more, Lizzie can stop on under a long engagement if she
will undertake to squelch the dissatisfied milkmaid and
the preposterous butcher, who are continually obtruding
last year’s accounts into the otherwise peaceful routine of
our daily life.

John Roberts made a spot-barred break of 363
lately, and Peall giving Dawson 3000 in 10,000, compiled
3304 the biggest “all in” break on record. As a conse-
quence, during two innings, the Huddersfield man did not
get a chance to take up the cue. Curiously enough,
Peall made a break of 2413 on the same (Nov. 4) four years
ago.
Fight On An Engine.
THE ENGINEER AND FIREMAN GRAPPLE IN A
DEATH STRUGGLE.
A fight to the death between the engineer and
fireman of an express train, speeding through the
country at night, is a bold conception of a modern
novelist.
The fireman is jealous of the engineer. One
night crazed with drink, he heaps on coal fuel
until the boiler is likely to explode. The engineer
pleads with him, then expostulates, and at last
attempts to prevent further addition. Then the
fireman grapples the engineer and tries to throw
him from the engine; a terrible struggle ensues.
The fight is for life ! They speak no. word, but
with teeth clenched, strive one to precipitate the
other to the ground. Meanwhile the train rushes
on. The engineer, finally growing weak, endea-
vours to reach the regulator to stop the train
and summon help. Too late! The crazed fire-
man guesses his plan, stiffens himself to a super-
human effort, lifts the exhausted engineer from
his feet, exclaiming : —“ Ah. you want to stop the
train. Now, out you go.” With this he flung the
engineer out; but the latter clings to him ; he
cannot shake him off; so both go out together.
Drawn under the wheels, they are found headless,
two bloody trunks clinging in a death embrace !
And the train, ungoverned, rushes on in the dark-
ness ! Hei’e the story ends ; the fate of the train
is left to the imagination. It is easy to see that
the chances are a hundred to one it rushes on to
destruction. So with the person having chronic
kidney complaint—it is almost certain to result in
Bright’s disease, then death, for the doctors admit
they have no cure for it. But a certain cure may
be had. Note what Robert Moore, of Horsham,
Victoria, says, under date of 20th August, 1890 1 —
‘"For a number of years I have suffered from
disease of the kidneys, which was gradually
undermining ray health. My urine was diseased,
and contained albumen in such quantities which
proved that I was suffering from the dreaded
Bright’s disease of the kidneys. As the disorder
advanced I became weaker, the knee joints, as
well as the ankles and eyelids, began to swell. I
lost all appetite, and experienced extreme wake-
fulness, nervousness, and finally, burning sen-
sations in the back. My urine was scanty, and
micturation was attended with much pain. When
commencing Warner’s safe cure and safe pills, I
had nearly lost my eyesight, but a dozen bottles
[removed the swelling of the joints and pave me
back my sight. In time the albumen all disap-
peared, and I recovered my health.” Mrs. E.
Payne, of Olive-street, Albury, N.S.W., writes as
follows : —‘ 1 For the past three years I have been
the subject of a most distressing disease of the
kidneys. At first I felt myself getting daily
weaker ; there were no particular pains, but some
twelve months’ since, [fearful pains invaded all
parts of my system. Ultimately dropsy set in,
and my stomach became preternaturally extended
and filled with water, literally bringing me to the
brink of the grave. An analysis of my urine
showed a large percentage of albumen, proving
that I was stricken with the dread Bright's disease.
I commenced the use of Warner’s safe cure, and
after I had taken one full bottle, the kidneys
resumed their action and the dropsy abated. Prior
to taking Warner’s safe cure the water had to be
removed by surgical means. Subsequent analysis
also proved that the albumen was lessening. To
be brief, I may add that I have now used eight or
nine bottles of safe cure, and every trace of this
grave and so called fatal disease has been re-
moved.”

WHAT IT IS.

Ist. It is in one word a cure ;it is not merely a

dues, heals, and cures ; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the vyhole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not merely irritate the outer
surface, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action a superior
curative virtue is superadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently; search-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
6th. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
SOME AUSTRALIAN EVIDENCE.
The editor of that favorably known and pro-
gressive periodica], the Illustrated Sydney
News, writes that he “has, much pleasure in
giving the proprietors of St. Jacobs oil his testi-
mony with respect to its wonderful curative
properties. The editor has suffered acutely with
chronic sciatica, and has found the greatest relief
by the application of St. Jacobs oil to the affected
parts, and has no hesitation in recommending it
to sufferers."’
The lady who edits the Band of Mercy
(Sydney), also states that she “can testify to the
value of St. Jacobs oil, as it is used in her family
with great benefit.”

Sydney TXT. holds that Parkes’ local-
option proposal, providing that the local optionists
of a district must secure a majority at the polls of
two votes to one ere their wishes are allowed to
take effect, is unfair, and that the local-option
party should be allowed to have their own way on
securing a bare majority as on any ordinary
question. The Teucohaph thus makes no dis-
tinction between an ordinary state question and
one so peculiarly involving matters of personal
liberty.
Wolfe’s Schnapps—It is grand.
The widow of the late Major Whyfcc-
Melville (now wife of Matkimonial News
Duncan) after the death of the sporting writer in
the hunting field, espoused an ex-fishmonger in
Dublin (the divorce of a Yankee wife) named
Higginson, who assumed the name Whyte-
Melville ” after marriage. The reverend gent —ho
had started in business as a parson—was after-
wards shot at while in a cab by an Irish clergy-
man whose daughter he had ruined. Mrs. Whyte-
Melville got a divorce after losing some thousands
through this adventurer
If in need of a stimulant take Wolfe’s Schnapps,
Count Festetics, the Anglo-Hungarian
snortsman, declined to receive Baron Hirsch,
whom “ Tummy” proposed to bring with him on
a visit to the Count. Consequently the Heir
Apparent’s self-invitation was allowed to drop,
and everybody is wondering how much he gets
per annum for maintaining a friendship which
exposes him to undisguised insult. And what a
crowd of snobs they are ! The “ social ” objection
to Hirsch is that lie continues to call himself a
Jew and appears to have more brains than any
ten of the “ old nobility.” But as he devotes a
lot of his intellect to toadying to Tummy, perhaps
after all the hereditary snob Festetics was
justified according to his own idiotic lights, in
refusing to have such a man at his table.
Wolfe’s Schnapps, the purest spirit procurable
Superintendent Have, in his Autobio-
graphy, has published an extra long chapter
about the Kellys, embodying all he has gleaned in
the ten years since. Ned Kelly, when young, as
henchman to Power, got the reputation of a
coward, but became a brilliant example of bush-
ranging education. Hare exonerates Standish,
who urged the Government to arm up the
troopers with proper carbines, and greatly
strengthen the Greta force, before the Kellys
broke out with the police murders. But for the
weakness of the officer in charge of Wangaratta,
the gang, thoroughly pumped out, would easily
have been taken alive, after this affair. Byrne and
Hart were unwittingly drawn into the murder
business of Sergeant Kennedy and Troopers
Scanlan and Lonergan, when MTntyre escaped,
by springing on Kennedy’s horse, which had
galloped away, after Kennedy screened himself
behind it, to fire on the bushrangers. Sherritt
told Hare that Ned told him that he and Dan
made Byrne and Hart kill Kennedy, because they
had no actual share in killing Scanlan and
Lonergan, wherefore they might have turned in-
formers. Hare excuses MTntyre altogether, for
if he had been murdered, the gang might have
burnt all the bodies, and never a word would be
known. Hare even calls MTntyre a brave man.
Wolfe’s Schnapps--popularity increases yearly
An American lady seeks divorce from her
husband on the grounds that he is continually
marrying her. He is a clergyman and a matri-
monial maniac, always seeking to be married,
though never wanting a new bride and always
afraid of flaws in the matrimonial contract. Con-
sequently their wedded life of eight years has
been one long honeymoon diversified by nine
trips to the altar. They were first married in a
Wisconsin Methodist church and were spending
the honeymoon in Dublin when Thomas dis-
covered that their union was not ecclesiasti-
cally perfect, and absented himself from the
nuntial couch until they were again made one in
St.'Patrick’s Cathedral. Inside six months a
flaw was discovered in the latest contract, and
another brief.separation was followed by avisit'to
a very High church. Marriages by Baptists,
Unitarians, Spiritualists, Presbyterians, and by
registrar followed in quick succession, and as
Thomas is a great stickler for forms and cere-
monies, the making-up of wedding-gowns, the
taste of wedding-cake, the fitting-on of rings-, the
selection of bridesmaids, the donning of orange-
blossoms and sempiternal honeymooning began to
pall on Stella. So when he proposed a visit to
the Salt Lake Tabernacle she revolted and issued
proceedings for divorce, declaring that what she
wanted was to settle down quietly and rear a
family, and not to rush round the world for ever
with a ridiculous man who was always behaving
as if they had been married yesterday, or were
about to be married to-morrow. Thomas is
weakening. He declares if she consents to
marriage in a Congregational church that it shall
positively be the last time he’ll ask her to marry
him.

remedy for constipation, biliousness, sick-head-
acbe, dyspepsia, jaundice, and the common
derangements of stomach, liver, and bowels.
They break up colds, mitigate fevers, relieve
gout, rheumatism, and neuralgia, cleanse the
system of malarial poison, and assist in the
formation of pure, rich blood. These -well-
known pills are compounded of the essential
virtues of the best vegetable cathartics, and
contain neither calomel nor any other deleteri-
ous drug. They may, therefore, be adminis-
tered in all cases where a purgative is needed,
without fear of ill effects. Being sugar-coated,
they are pleasant to take, and their medicinal
strength is preserved for an indefinite period
and in any climate. They should be in every
domestic medicine chest and traveller’s satchel.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will he paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under :
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. ( Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especiall%
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5, Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. Ihe
name and, address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not mereli on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
tvrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

What we are Prepared to do.
We are prepared to supply 100,000'of the Australa-
sian public with samples of FRAZER’S SULPHUR
TABLETS, gratis and post free.
A Word of Advice.
Don’t write for the samples simply because we offer
them free of charge, nor because your neighbour does.
Read our advertisement through to the end, and then
decide whether you or yours need or would be benefited
by the use of FRAZER’S SULPHUR TABLETS.
We do not pose as philanthropists. This is a business
offer. We have an article that we know from severe
tests and from results achieved will sell on its merits if
seen, tasted and tested. Sulphur is the oldest, the best,
and safest remedy for the blood, for the skin, for the
connexion, for rheumatism, and for constipation.
FRAZER/S SULPHUR TABLETS are composed of
sulphur incorporated with cream of tartar and other
curative ingredients. They are much more efficacious
than sulphur in milk or treacle. This has been proved
time and again. They are liked. Men favour them,
women hail them as a boon, and children think them as
pleasant to eat as confectionary. They benefit all, and
are as efficacious and curative as they are safe and agree-
able.
Their Success.
“ FRAZER’S SULPHUR TABLETS” are the
latest and greatest British success. They have so much
merit as to always do good. They are so palatable and
agreeable that a child will take them with delight. They
are so safe that the weakest person may take them with-
out injury. They benefit every function of the body by
tonic action on the blood vessels.
Why do we take this Risk?
This is what people ask us. We reply, because
those who write for the samples afterwards buy packets
of FRAZER’S SULPHUR TABLETS by post from
us, or obtain them from chemists, stores, or medicine
vendors. People likewise recommend and keep the
Tablets by them. The consequence is that we sell several
hundred dozen packets weekly, and the sale is rapidly
growing. This is trade by merit. The public have
shown discrimnation, and that they can appreciate merit,
and it was in firm reliance that this would be the case
that we were led to incur this risk.
Do 1 need them ?
If you have Rheumatism, Yes. They will kill the
decomposed and poisonous excess of uric acid in the blood
causing the disease.
If you have Constipation, Yes, They are a gentle,
safe, and a simple laxative, as suitable for children (in
smaller doses) as for adults.
If you have Hemorrhoids, Yes. They relieve the
congestion of venous blood at the affected parts, and
afford relief.
If you have Ulcers, Scrofula, Scurvy, or other
impurities of the blood, Yes. They neutralise the blood
poisons causing the diseases and expel them out of the
system.
If you have Skin Disease or Eruption, Yes. They
have a specific curative effect on all such, as they open
the pores from internally, and free them from obstruction
and disease taint. They likewise control where they do
not kill the disease principle from the blood, which causes
Eczema and other diseases.

At this season of the year it is usual in all grades
of life to call a halt, as it were, in order to look
down the vista of the bygone year and over
the devious and profitable or unprofitable path
traversed during that period in our life’s journey.
It is now that one recognises most keenly the
failures of the past and determines, while making
new plans, to avoid errors in the year to come.
Here it is that the surveyor of the chain of past
events pauses to bury his past blunders and
to erect over them a sort of trigonometrical
cairn of wasted opportunities. But all have
not thus to bemoan days that have fled
never to return. Here is a case in point.
Sydney for years has been looked down on as a
sort of Sleepy Hollow by the more commercial
and speculative community of Melbourne, but
day by day that reproach is being rendered null
by the enterprise of a few of her citizens who
seem determined to make things hum in the love*
best of Australian cities. This enterprise is most
unmistakeably evidenced in the demolition of old
buildings and the erection of what may be justly
styled palaces in every part of the city, for
one of the maxims of the progressive business
men of the age seems to be that new goods cannot
be produced or distributed to advantage from
old buildings. Fired with this idea Mr. W. E.
Smith a few weeks back opened his new whole-
sale stationery, printing, and bookbinding estab-
lishment in Bridge-street. Fifteen years ago
Mr. Smith, a native of England, embarked his
savings in a venture out of which his present
business has grown, and by unremitting attention
to business and the exercise of keen business
faculties his business ever kept extending, until
its requirements demanded a new setting. From
this demand was evoked the present lofty and
substantial erection for the master-mind of the
stationery trade on land owned by him in Bridge-
street near Pitt-street. The new structure has a
frontage to Bridge-street of over 50ft., with a
height of over 100 ft., and is built mainly of free-
stone, with red brick dressings about the win-
dows, and has been constructed of such stout
materials that, despite the use of much heavy,
constantly-moving machinery, there is absolutely
no vibration to be felt on any of the numerous
floors. The building is divided into seven fiats, o
which we append the dimensions :—Basement
2770 ft.; Ground Floor, 2712 ft. ; First, 2736 ft.
Second, 2786 ft. ; Third, 2786 ft. ; Fourth, 2786 ft. .
Fifth, 2843 ft.; Total square feet, 19,419. The
top floor, the seventh, is the receptacle of the
banking goods, and here are stored reference
copies of all the important work done for the
various banks, syndicates, and private firms
throughout Australia. The next floor (ruling,
binding and paging, &e.) is the most interesting.
Here a ledger or cheque book may be followed
from its initial stage as paper or leather till ib
becomes the finished volume, fit for the desk of the
accountant. Here we have six ruling machines,
one of which, the latest, performs the most
intricate kinds of ruling with a minimum amount
of manual control. Here are several guillotines,
huge razor-edged cutters by various makers that
pare with ease and celerity the rough edges from
large masses of paper. Here also are to be seen
bevelling and backing machines, envelope and label
cutters, three wire stitchers, half-a-dozen embossing
machines (one self-cleaning), a mill-board cutting
machine, someblocking presses of tremendous power,
and perforating, numbering, paging and indexing
machines. Here is also performed, by hand, the
simple and beautiful process of marbling book*
edges. On the next floor downwards, the
fifth, we have the composing-room; here the
type is set, also where the copper-plate work
(a tedious hand process) is done, the litho-
graphing by hand-presses (the stones are dried
by atmospheric gas, an innovation introduced for
time-saving purposes), and the Htho - stone
polishing. Here is also placed the private room
where the Sydney University examination-
papers are turned out. On the fourth floor
we find at work the letterpress and litho-
printing machines, some of which are work-
ing in colours ; stereo and planing machines,
and a wonderful machine Mr. Smith’s own
invention (its patenting cost £1000), which
turn out 40,000 cheque-forms in a day numbered
in correct rotation, and can consecutively print
numbers from 1 to 1,000,000 without interruption.
It is almost impossible to estimate the amount of
time and labour saved by the introduction of this
apparently complicated piece of mechanism.
Looking at it one becomes impressed with the idea
that there are no limits to human ingenuity, and
begins to anticipate the coming of the day when
the bulk of the world’s work will be done by a few
nimble-fingered inventors who will sit - down at
occasional intervals to play on a lettered key-
board, each of whose symbols when touched seta
in motion some docile but terribly-powerful mam-
moth of machinery. But in those days capital
will most likely be nationalised, and such able
organisers and directors as Mr. Smith will simply
act for the nation as generals of the great army of
industry. But to return to the subject on hand. On
the third floor the paper and leather is stored, ready
for conversion into books and other business
requisites; here are working with almost noiseless
rapidity two large gas-engines by Crossley, of
Manchester, capable of working up to 50-horse
power, with which the greater part of the
machinery throughout the building is kept in
motion. On the ground floor are placed the retail
department, pen-room, travellers’ room, counting-
house, lift and staircase. In the base-
ment (a cool, airy, clean, and dry apartment)
is the packing department and ink-room,
and here the massiveness of the structure
and the strength of the beams and pillars may be
noted. It will be interesting to note the number
machines constantly at work in this hive of
industry :—l5 Machines in Letterpress Depart-
ment ; 17 machines in Lithographic Department;
55 machines and presses in Binding Department;
2 12-h.p. Gas Engines ; 245 ft. of Shafting ; 980 ft.
of Belting. About 100 employees are engaged
under the supervision of five managers in the
various departments, and by their hands over
80 machines are kept continuously working,
Each floor is exceptionally well lighted and venti*
lated, and on every hand are seen traces of
excellent management and completeness, Electric
bells, hydrants and speaking-tubes, and every
precaution against fire, and for the extinction of
fire; the latest and most approved means of
communication between different parts of the
building, and other appliances whereby business
may be expedited, are visible on every flat, and
the stock-in-trade, like the structure, is of the
best. Indeed, the machinery and stock bear a
valuation of over £70,000. As an indication
of the massiveness and stability of the build-
ing, it is interesting to note that, though the
large engines are 30ft. above the basement, abso-
lutely no vibration is felt, even when the
machinery on the third floor is in motion. The
prevailing impression after making a visit of in-
spection to Mr. Smith’s establishment is that no
expense has been spared in getting together the
most complete plant and providing that work
shall be carried on under the best possible
oonditions. It is also made evident, when the
names of the firm’s constituents are noted, that it
has earned the confidence of the great bulk of the
business people, not only in this but in other
provinces, and that the business, large though it
be, is but the germ of the mammoth concern
which must result from the rapid increase
in local population and production. Be
that as it may, however, Mr. Smith
seems to have fathomed the problem of success,
For it is evident that the mere possession of
money is not alone sufficient to ensure the success-
ful carrying on of any commercial industry, as
the numerous enormous failures "in every line of
industry bear testimony. Few men who started
in business inside the last twenty years with a
limited capital can point with justifiable pride to
a prosperous business quietly and painstakingly
reared on such unpretentious foundations ; there
fore it is evident that the faculties necessary to
the uprearing and maintenance of great mer-
cantile enterprises are scarce ; the methods,
either unknown, or difficult of acquirement,
His recipe, doubtless, would be unflagging in-
dustry, punctuality, intelligent supervision, good
wages, and the employment of all the latest
appliances that genius can invent and science
construct, indeed, there could be no greater
treat for those who have a mechanical turn, than
to take a trip through this establishment, to view
the latest modern appliances which have grown
out of the primitive methods of Guttenberg,
Caxton and Franklin. Dazed and astounded by
a close inspection of the ramifications of this
palace of art and industry, one is inclined to
receive with incredibility the statement of the
calm and unassuming proprietor that it is all
the work of his hand and brain, and that he has
managed single-handed to build up a business
vhose ramifications extend to all parts of Aus-
tralasia, and second to none of its class m the
Southern Hemisphere.

Old King Capital.
OR, JACK AND THE QUEEN’S TALK.
AN EXTRAVAGANZA IN THREE ACTS.
(BY EDMUND FISHER.)
CHARACTERS :
Old King Capital— A mighty monarch, but a
filthy looker when seen very olo.se.
Queen Delicate— His lawful wife, ivho was
bought by the POUND.
Pompumpus —The King’s Chamberlain, a haughty ,
but rather naughty personage.
Swagger Swigger— Chief Man-in-Buckram, a
costly warrior who charges the people.
Blackleg —The King’s favourite slave, a man of (/)
colour for everything.
Jack Hardgraft— The Natural Enemy of 0.K.C.,
Leader of the Tribes of Toil.
Fairy Bulletin —The Genius of Honest Labor.
Soldiers, Capitalists (old), Society-belles, Toilers,
Fairies, Ac., Ac.
Scene I— Marble Hall in the Palace of O.K.
Capital. Blackleg discovered chained to
the leg of a gorgeous throne at back of stage.
Music.
Blackleg—
Behold a slave, a caterpillar, worm !
I crawl along through life’s allotted term
Devoid of honour, manly pluck, or nerve,
Despised alike by them I thwart or serve.
By me be warned how low a chap may sink
Who oft lies idle, idolising drink.
While sober men are working for their bread
Base Blackleg hides his not diminished head
(Tis mostly swollen) and reviles his luck.
But when for higher wages they have struck
I sober up, on hearing of the news,
And take the work they beg me to refuse.
Vain are the efforts of my sturdy neighbour
To set me thinking of the rights of Labour.
What matters if it be of rights bereft ?
“ Good job ! ” I say, and go for what is left
Until I’m tired, or, others coming back,
I get re-tired—in plainer words, “the sack.”
(Pompumpus enters, and B. grovels.)
Low at thy feet thy slave is crouching, see.
Pompumpus—
A slave ! Absurd ! Please understand you’re free—
To call you so our daily press engages,
As your reward for keeping down the wages.
Yes, free as air, or as the rolling main.
(Aside) That’s why he, like a watchdog, wears a
chain.
(Again addressing B.) My honest friend, maintain
your pecker, pray—
(sounds of music without)
Here comes the King—now, Hip-hip-hip-Hooray !
Enter Soldiers, Policemen and Monopolists
(carrying special-constable batons) followed by the
King, with Swigger Swagger, Pages, Ac., in
attendance. King takes scat on Throne, and pats
Blackleg’s check, who licks the royal hand. Pom-
pumpus gets through the usual bowing, in the course
of which ceremonies he mutters, "It’s a dam had
’buse, ain’t it ?” and surreptitiously spits, pre-
imratory to
Song—Pompumpus and Chorus.
King Capital sits on his golden throne
And gloats o’er his ill-got greed ;
He laughs Ha, Ha ! when the toilers groan,
And mocks at the poor man’s need.
Chorus —The preposterous poor man’s need.
Great Capital sits on his throne of gold,
A monarch by Satan’s grace,
His joys are born of the griefs untold
Of the bulk of the human race.
Chorus —The dregs of the human race.
O, never a King so great as he
Has dazzled the eyes of fools;
In every land, on every sea
That sovereign mighty rules —
Each side of the line he rules;
New customs grow whilst old decay,
Fresh nations rise and fall;
And every dog must have his day,
But Capital lives and reigns alway
Tyrannical King of all —
Of wicked, and good, and all.
Capital—
Bless you, my subjects, ever most devoted !
Pompumpus—
Your Majesty, we hope we find you bloated.
Capital—
Yaas, pretty fairly ; things are looking up
And trade is brisker since the Melbourne Cup.
{Turning to S. S. ) —Of course you went for Carbine,
gallant Swagger ?
Swag Swig—
Your Majesty, I pawned my sword and dagger,
And having put those weapons up the spout.
On Musket’s son I laid the money out.
Capital—
A merry jest, indeed —but stay ! dimnation !
You didn’t leave the goods with your relation—
You took ’em out, we trust ? Your ready sabre
We look to ever in our wars with Labour.
Swag Swig—
Your Majesty has little cause for fear
I know my duty, and I have them here. {Pro-
duces 'whole armoury of implements of destruc-
tion. King descends from Throne and slaps
him on shoulder; they all advance to footlights,)
Song—Swag Swig, King and Chorus.
Swag Swig—
My name is Swagger Swigger,
I’ve a tall, commanding figger,
On the mob I pull the trigger
When your Gracious deigns to nod;
We have come to pretty passes
When the nasty, vulgar masses
Dare defy the monied classes—
Like their impudence, bigod !
Capital—
-0 ! the chief amongst the sources
Of our happiness, of course, is
Found in military forces—
The defenders of the sod.
Chorus—
Our fiery defenders
Of the grabbers of the sod.
{March round.)
Swag Swig—
When invaders a/en’t invading
1 am drilling arfl parading,
Or at Court a-masqueradmg
In my uniform so rare:
But when the Labour faction
Shows the least dissatisfaction,
Then I summon troops to action
And for civil war prepare.
Capital—
The kettle-drums start beating,
And the rifles (new repeating)
Play old Harry with the “ meeting ”
Ere the mob can say a prayer—
Chorus—
And the fragments of the meeting
Are sent flying through the air.
(Soldiers from square, with King in the centre.
Everybody else dances, except Blackleg, who gaily
rattles his chain. Tableau ! Soldiers manoeuvre,
and King returns to the Throne.)
Capital—
And yet I am not happy, not content,
Despite my hoard of unearned increment.
I keep rebellion down with sword and sawder,
And laws intended to preserve the hoarder —
And yet, and yet, my heart of hearts within,
I am afraid of Fairy Bulletin.
For ten long years—’twas 80 she was born in—
That frisky fay my side has been a thorn in;
Deeper and deeper still the spike is sticking —
Not thus the sides of my intent need pricking.
I shall be forced at last, in desperation,
To have her blood and stop her circulation.
Swag Swig ( kneeling before Throne) —
Say but the word and I all danger scorning,
(While he is kneeling Fairy Bulletin comes up
through the floor. She is dressed in a spotless
white robe and carries a glittering staff. Swagger
Swigger, not observing the vision, continues)
Will mash her fairy staff, and (F. B. taps him
on shoulder) O ! good morning.
{Exit Swagger Swigger hastily.)
Consternation in Court. Fairy Bulletin writes
with her staff a message in Volapuk upon the wall,
the characters stand out against the marble in blue
fire. )
Fairy Bulletin (chanting)—
See the writing on the wall
Of this royal marble hall.
Let another Daniel tell
What those flaming letters spell.
More of me
You shall see—
For the present, King, farewell!
{Exit down trap.)
Pompumpus (slyly smacking his Ups) —
Disgraceful person ! (aside) though, in fact, I’m
smitten,
(To King) Pay no attention, Sire, to what she’s
written.
I’ll rub it out and substitute fresh phrases ;
(Attempts to rub it out, then shrieks, and rubs his
hands in agony)
I’m burnt alive ! O, H ! I should say blazes.
Capital—
My poor Potnpumpus, let the nonsense rest—
Pompumpus—
Thanks, Gracious King, I think it would be best;
The meaning of those words we haven’t got;
One fact alone is plain—they’re awful hot.
However, I’ll translate ’em at my leisure.
And now, a dance, if ’tis your royal pleasure.
('King nods , and Pom. claps his hands.)
(Enter bevy of Society Belles singing.)
Ring the merry bells in marriage,
Ancient gentlemen of wealth
Beauty begs your hand and carriage—
Never mind your failing health.
Damsels once thought much of Cupid,
’Twas of love they used to sing ;
Now-a-days we’re not so stupid—
Ring the belles ! Ring the belles !
Now-a-days we’re not so stupid—
Money, money is the thing.
We have heard plebeian scoffers
Say that maids are bought and sold
Just as lambs, to whoso offers
Biggest price in lands and gold.
If that be, then choose the daughter
Of a well-connected dame,
Lead her like a lamb for slaughter—
Ring the belles ! Ring the belles !
Lead her like a lamb for slaughter,
To a life of “ honoured ” shame.
(A mazy dance: the old Capitalists offer cheque-
books, which the girls at first affect to refuse,
hiding their blushes behind their fans as they
glide around the stage. Dance concludes with
each venerable gent dropping on one knee at the
feet of his particular Belle, who accepts a cheque.
Tableau, Curtain.)
ACT 11.
Scene. —On the outskirts of a public domain*
Distant view of Habdgkaft’s three-roomed
dwelling-place.
“ The house was a detached one,
The outside old and mean,
And naught within those cardboard walls
The audience ever seen.”
Beautiful sunset, at the termination of which a
wild, towzled-looking person, with a paint-brush
behind his ear, appears at prompt side of the
stage and boxes his acknowledgments of the ap-
plause. Then enter an odd lot of fairies.
Chorus.
The weary day of toil is ended,
And the worker rests awhile;
Hither we our way have wended,
To receive him with a smile.
Lightly tripping come the fairies,
Singing sweeter than canaries,
Where we are there never care is—
Sorrow flies from fairy smile.
(Fairies break out into a gruesome, mechanical
grin, that makes even the gallery-boys shudder.)
Grand Ballet.
Enter Jack Hardgraft, carrying hag of tools
across his shoulder. Fairies smile again and
exit, to be informed by the stage-manager that
there isn't a, blanked woman in the whole crowd
knows how to shake a leg.
Hardgraft (putting down bag of tools) —
I’m tired out; eight hours upon the job
I’ve fairly been, and only earned eight “ bob.”
One “ bob” an hour! For sixty minutes toiling
Twelve pence to keep my humble kettle boiling!
It isn’t much ; the price is not appalling,
And yet, alas! it stands a chance of falling.
When trade is bad “sacked” labour bears the
losses,
And when ’tis good the profits are the boss’s.

S
ytinricA /c,
Of this hard fact each moment makes us surer —
The rich grow richer, and the w-richeA poorer ;
The pudding-maker oft from hunger suffers,
While all the plums appear to su-it duffers.
Ha, ha !no matter —I must to the cottage
To get my berth right, and prepare some potage.
(He picks up the tools and is about to move
off, when Queen Delicate appears, attended by
Sassiety Belles.)
Delicate— Pray pardon this intrusion, sir, I beg—
(pretended aside)
I must admit he has a lovely leg.
Hardgraet—
What is your business, madam ? By your dress
You cannot like the likes of me, I guess.
Delicate —O ! you mistake
Hardgraet — No miss I think of taking>
Nor want a queen, although my head is a-king’
(rubs his forehead wearily.)
Please let me know the object of your visit
With all dis-patch (points scornfully to the
Queen’s “make-up”) and powder—well,
what is it?
Delicate (coquettishly) —
Impatient thing! you make me feel quite
frightened—
Sit here, by me, and you shall be enlightened.
(Sinks gracefully upon a fallen tree-trunk and
beckons to Hardgraet, who sulkily joins her.
Sassiety Belles cough and retire out of (stage) ear-
shot. Queen taps Hardgraet with her fan and
■resumes.)
On working-men quite awfully I dote;
Come closer —there—now promise me to vote
For Tories only at the next election,
And ne’er to raise another insurrection :
The last one failed —I wish not to upbraid—
But think of all the harm you did to trade,
Although I must admit that, on the whole,
We made a little by the rise in coal.
(Tickles his chin.)
Now do be good, and don’t again rebel
Against the King, who loves you all so well—
Be loyal to the interests of Capital (presses close
to him).
Hardgraet (rising ) —
You can’t get fooling round this simple chap
at all,
Your eyes and jewels in the pan are flashing,
A “ bi g-pot” hater doesn’t like the mashing
Of artful, high-bred petticoat intriguers ;
Fur long I’ve had my eye on Primrose Leaguers,
Yes, all my eye you feather-brained decoys on,
And I despise you worse, much worse, than
poison.
Delicate ( coming after him ) —
Bad, naughty man ! how shockingly pervorsey—
You don’t show much respect for Lady Jersey.
Duet—Delicate and Hardgraet.
Delicate—
Prithee, let me coax and wheedle
Asa Primrose Leaguer can—
Hardgraet—
Go, or I shall get the needle,
I’m a sturdy working-man—
Delicate—
See, my fingers gently press yours,
In your honest eyes I stare —
Hardgraet—
Though you bade my lips caress yours,
Madam, I should merely swear.
Delicate— Much you grieve me..
Hardgraet— Can’t you leave me ?
Take your hand from off my coat—
Useless staying—
Delicate — Hear me praying
For your interest and vote,
(Both together) —
For
/your)
I my /
interest and vote.
(Queen curtsies, and Haedgeaet expresses h’ack
and bitter scorn.)
Delicate—
Let me spin my pretty story
In my toney tempting way—
Haedgeaet—
All your arts, fair lady Tory,
Quite in vain you bring to play—
Delicate—
See, I rub against your shoulder,
That’s a very fetching trick —
Haedgeaet—
E’en were your advances bolder
You would merely make me sick.
Delicate — You displease me.
Haedgeaet—Bah ! you tease me.
I don’t care a jot or groat,
For your smirking—
Delicate —I am working
For your interest and vote,
( Together)—
For
/ your)
I my j
interest and vote.
(Repeat Business.)
Delicate (pleading)—
Art still so cruel, still so cold, so icy ?
With me ’tis mostly veni, vidi, vici—
Haedgeaet—
Well, mostly vice y, all might say who’ve seen us—
Be this your motto Queeney, vicey Venus—
Delicate (snorting) —
Ungrateful creature, loathsome, low-bred clown >
Thank Heaven for our laws that keep you down !
(Summons the Society Belles, and is about to
flounce off when F. Bulletin emerges from the
trunk of a large tree.)
F. Bulletin (whispers to Haedgeaet)—
You’ve had a visit from this perfumed smilah—
Did Sampson hold his own against Delilah?
Haedgeaet (whispers) —
To lose her head I’ve lured the fowler fair on—
But I’m as right as Moses, with my hair on.
F. Bulletin—
I didn’t doubt it— (turning to Delicate). Just a
moment, Queen,
Ere you return to grace the festive scene—
I do beseech you, give your friends a hint
To keep from games that don’t look well in print.
But almost tempt poor, simple Tom and Biddy
To cry—’Tis money makes the mare go—giddy.
At dames of fashion Traviata scoffs.
“She ain’t no worse,” she says, “than lots of
toffs.”
I often weep, although the thoughtless laugh,
O’ er sins revealed by my divining-staff;
I hear of bruised hearts and broken vows,
And ructions ending in vice-regal rows ;
Of pretty matrons and young single girls
With lives embittered by the suites of earls.
When toffy suites a midnight thumping cotoh,
The process turns them into batter-Scotch ;
’Tis lucky, though, that heads are mighty thick
Which learn love’s lesson from a black wood stick.
But I’m digressing. Tell your friends polite
To keep their characters, like hands, quite white.
There’s too much courting in your circle courtly.
Adieu, so long ! Expect to see me shortly.
Delicate—
Audacious cat ! to talk like this —how dare you 1
No good, will come of the ill-will I bear you.
(Exit Queen and Attendants.)
F. Bulletin—
So much for her (snaps fingers), and now, to come
to biz. :
To crush the King it my intention is,
And make his bloated gang divide the spoil
From this time forth with all the tribes of Toil.
To-morrow night be ready, say at ten,
To make a swoop with fifty million men
Upon the Palace.
Hardgraet — Will his troops stand fast •
F. Bulletin —
No fear, your cause is theirs they’ve found at last.
No more will they give heed to Swagger Swigger,
But greet his orders with a mocking snigger;
The rank and file—on this I’m game to wager—
Won’t shoot a miner to oblige a maw.
Now understand, at ten o’clock, and this’ll
The signal be to swoop—l’ll blow my whistle.
(Blows whistle. Enter all the Fairies and a
few Toilers carrying implements of trade. The
latter dodge among the trees and endeavour to
look like a great multitude. F.B. takes hold of
H’s arm and leads him to the footlights, after the
manner of stage conspirators, The others follo w
slowly and stealthily.)
Song {sotto voce)— Fairy B. and Chorus.
F.B. —At the witching hour of ten
Come with fifty million men—
Chorus — At the witching hour of ten
He, with fifty million men,
Has to come, and—well, what then ?
F.B. —Come around the Palace gate,
Patiently awhile to wait —
Chorus — How long will we have to wait ?
F.B.—Till my whistle shrill I blow
Then like light’ning in you go—
Chorus — Yes, by thunder !in we’ll go.
F.B. —Like the whirlwind fly upstairs,
Take His Gracious unawares—
Chorus — Goodness gracious ! unawares ?
F.B. —All the rest I’ve neatly planned.
Trust to me, you understand ?
Chorus — Oh, of course, we understand.
{Pale stage moon rises at the rate of six inches per
second.)
Hardgraet {bending reverently to P. 8., and
chanting) —
Now, by yonder moon I swear
With my army to be there —
Swear by all the snobs in Hell—
By the ladder on which Parnell
Used to secretly retiah
From the chamber of Maria ;
Swear by Scott’s appointed seat,
Where he ought to sit at meat —
By the bounding ocean blue,
By the legs of Kyrle Bellew;
By the coming Jersey pair—
By Sir ’Enry’s ’ead of ’air—
Here, upon my knees, I swear.
{Kneels.)
{Fairies hop feebly around HARDGRAET whilst
Toilers perform weird mystic evolutions right and
left. F.B. places laurel wreath upon Hahdgrapt’s
brow and points to pale moon, which changes into a
silvery horse-shoe. Curtain.)
ACT 111.
Scene.— Hall in K. Capital’s Palace (as before).
Court ball in full swing—Capitalists and
Belles dancing minuet. Kino and Queen
seated in couch in foreground with Swag
Swig in attendance. Blackleg is still chained
to the leg of the Throne. Soldiers and servants
in greater or less profusion, according to how
the show has been doing lately. F. Bulle-
tin’s writing still gleams on the wall. Music
ceases.
Enter Pompumpus, who advances to O. K.
Capital.
Pompumpus—
Great Sire, may your shadow ne’er grow less !
I’ve just been fixing up the Daily Press
With Foreign News —concocting Tory fables
That duly blossom forth as Latest Cables —
“Disgraceful charge against the Irish Party,”
“ The Grand Old Man found cheating at Ecarte,”
“ More Noble Actions by the Prince of Wales ”
And so forth—but you know the kind of tales.
Capital—
Thanks very much—to-morrow morning sing
Them very softly to your Gracious King :
Of classic music far too much I’ve had,
I pay to hear it, and it makes me bad,
But sweeter melodies one can’t desire
Than those extracted from the Cable lyre.
{To Delicate, who has made a tremendous dis-
play of being lost in thought. )
You seem quite pensive, my expensive Queen —
Delicate —
I’m thinking, ah ! of all that might have been.
Song—Queen Delicate.
{This is interpolated, “ by special desire ”)
Beside the little babbling rill
I chase the buzzing bumble-bee,
The cockatoo his ballad shrill
Sings on the Eucalyptus tree—
The good old Eucalyptus tree;
I watch the yellow lizard sleep
Beneath the shade of fern fronds green—
The dream is o’er, and now I weep
For happiness that might have been.
Ah ! me. Ah ! me,
My life is dree.
Aweary is the mobled Queen
Who ne’er can know the ecstasy
Of happiness that might have been.
My crown of twenty-carat gold,
Bedotted round with mwels rare,
It is a brown straw bonnet old,
Such as the rural maidens wear—
The truly rural maidens wear :
My gown of print is cut quite plain,
My bare hands are not over-clean—
The dream is o’er, I weep again
For happiness that might have been.
Ah ! me, Ah ! me,
My life is dree.
Aweary is the mobled Queen, &c.
Capital—
A pretty song, but somewhat music-shoppy.

Delicate—
I draw a coin of fourpenoe on each copy
From Blank and Cos.
Capital— Egad ! upon my honour,
I never knew a primer prima-donna.
Come, Swagger-Swigger, let us join the dancers
And take our partners for the Sydney Lancers.
(King and Swag. Swig, choose two girls and lost
themselves in crowd.)
Pompumpus (to Queen)—
Young Hardgraft’s capture can Your Gracious
compass ?
Delicate —
Indeed I can’t, my excellent Pompumpus ;
These working men, except that thing (glances at
Blackleg) in fetters,
Are growing most uncivil to their betters;
My Primrose canvass surly Hardgraft spurns—
Pompum.—
No doubt he has been listening to Burns,
A dreadful person much adored by dockers,
Of whom you’ve read in penny daily shockers ;
His long clear head I’d dearly like tostampy on—
(Grinds heel on ground.)
Now, give me democrats like H. H. Champion.
Delicate—
O, he’s so nice, so calm, you know, and cool—
Says pretty things and gets his clothes from Poole;
He served us lately, and we must requite him—
Remind me (rising) that I ask the King to knight
him.
Duet—Queen and Pompumpus.
Pompum.—
Rads, there are advanced and torrid,
Holding all men equal quite—
Delicate—
Rads, like those are really horrid,
Most abhorrent in my sight
Pom. —We’ve a democrat globe-trotter
Who will languidly discuss
Labour strikes, or La Belle Potter—
(Together)—
That’s the sort of Rad. for us—
That’s the sort of Rad. for us.
(Dance.)
Pompum. —Rads there are devoid of polish.
Reaping only as they’ve sown.
Delicate —We prefer the Rad tol-lollish
Born with “money of his own.”
Pompum. —One, a beast of simple habit,
Smokes a pipe upon a ’bus —
Delicate (drawls) —Let’s have weeds and Hansom-
cab-it,
Together —That’s the sort of Rad for us.
That’s the sort of Rad for us.
( Dance)
King and Swag. Swig, come forward again, and
King conducts Delicate to Throne. Blackleg,
who has been crouching underneath, now crawls out
and looks appealingly up to the King.
Capital—
Great Vanderbilt! Why, what’s the matter,
puny ’un ?
Blackleg—
I crave your leave to go and join a Union
Emancipate your slave you might, I think.
Capital—
Absurd 1 (To Pom.) Just give him all he wants to
drink —
Colonial whisky, that’s the stuff that makes
A man quite happy—
Pompum. — If he cares for snakes (exit).
Blackleg— Do let me go.
Capital—
I fear he has turned kicker.
I’ll have to drug him—ho ! there with the liquor.
(Pompumpus returns with jugful of blue-fire, but
Blackleg refuses to drink).
Blackleg—
Don’t tempt me, honoured sir, because I'm off it,
I’ve signed the pledge, and Munro is my prophet.
(King motions Pompum. to put down the cup, and
kicks Blackleg under the Throne again).
Capital—
Well, after all, the stuff is bad in one sense.
Pompumpus—
But, Sire, this moral fit’s all stuff and nonsense
(winks at orchestra).
Song.—Pompumpus.
Doubtless you will think my ditty
Stuff and Nonsense.
Neither very new nor. witty
Stuff and Nonsense.
Possibly beyond all bearing,
Anybody, I am preparing
Soon to hear you all declaring

Why are damsels “ weak ” on Sundays?
Stuff and Nonsense !
Special day of Mrs. Grundy’s,
Stuff and Nonsense.
Mother Eve she ate an apple,
And a girl who’s got a chap’ll
Take a long walk after chapel—
Stuff and Nonsense. Non-sense, Stuff !
Have the Melbourne folks got noses ?
Stuff and Nonsense !
Are their drains like tuberoses?
Stuff and Nonsense!
Of the Yarra water inky
Would a little glassful, thinkee,
Choke a black or kill a Chinkee ?
Stuff and Nonsense. Non-sense, Stuff !
Is our Parkes an Epic poet ?
Stuff and Nonsense !
When he drivels, don’t he go it?
Stuff and Nonsense !
Probably he sings to Babbie
How a shabbie, drabbie, cabbie
Nearly shut up Papa’s gabbie,
Stuff and Nonsense. Non-sense, Stuff!
Latterly we have been bearin’
Stuff and Nonsense
Talked about a son of Erin—
Stuff and Nonsense!
Are his enemies sinceah
In their sorrow for O’Sheah?
Is Parnell as bad as we, ah,
Stuff and Nonsense. Non-sense, Stuff!

Stuff and Nonsense!
When he found a near relation—
Stuff and Nonsense!
Had received an awful whop on
Parts were captings mostly flop on ?
Curtain here I’d better drop on
Stuff and Nonsense. Non-sense, Stuff!
(Whilst the Court is applauding—much more
vigorously than the people “in front ” — Fairy
Bulletin comes up through same old trap-door.
Consternation,)
Delicate—
Show that young woman out.
Capital — Dear, don’t be hasty.
Pompumpus (aside)—
He’d like to save her for himself—she's tasty.
Capital
This is the person who defaced our wall.
Swag-Swig—
It is—Oh ! let me like a soldier fall (faints, and is
removed to the hack).
F. Bulletin—
From Fairyland, O ! King, I have been sent
To change at last your form of Government.
(Mage manager at the icings strikes ten o’clock
on a saucepan lid.)
The hour has come, and now my message read.
(The letters on the wall emit such a brilliant
limelight that all, except Fairy 8., cover their
eyes with their hands. She blows a ivhistle.)
Capital— I’m blinded!
Delicate Dazzled !
Pompumpus— As for me, I’m and and !
(Enter Hardgraft and about two dozen of the
fifty million Toilers.)
Fairy B,—
Yon mystic message to this Court translate.
Hardgraft (reading message, the words of which
die out, one by one, and the company are able to
open their eyes again) —
Now King and King for good co-operate !
Hardgraft and Capital join hand in hand,
Each for the State, and for the State the Land ;
The young King active till, his work completed,
He sits where passive Capital was seated,
Who, passing on to restful regions rare,
Leaves Hardgraft’s son to claim the second chair;
And so, from age to age, two Kings shall reign,
While all men work with willing hand or brain,
With labour paying till, by labour paid,
The money-maker gets his money made.
F. Bulletin {to Hardgraft) —
Most worthy King, fidelity I swear !
Allow me (takes his hand and leads him to
Throne. Taps Delicate on shoulder)—
Let the King sit there f Delicate gives up her seat)
(to Capital) Monarch old in years remain,
Yet in mind be born again.
(waves staff over Capital’s head, and electric
lights appear all round his crown. Some evenings
the electric light doesn’t work properly.)
Capital (looking cherubic) —
Forgive my bloated follies of the past—
I’ve been a wrong ’un, but I’m right at last.
This is indeed a bloodless revolution :
(to Hardgraft)
We’ll pass an Act to change the Constitution,
And make it treason not to toil or spin—
Pompumpus—
This is too dreadful! Where do we come in ?
King Hardgraft—
Don’t fear, although you’ve never earned a crust,
You’ll find us much more generous than just.
(Pompumpus and capitalists look relieved.)
Release poor Blackleg (they do. B. kneels.)
Rise, misguided cuss,
Accept our pardon, and be one of us.
Keep off the lotion, and repent at leisure —
Blackleg —l do repent already, may it pleaseyer.
(Retires amongst toilers, who shake hands and
congratulate all round).
Hardgraft—
E’en Swagger-Swigger in our pardon now shares —
Boys, take their swords and make ’em into plough-
shares
(the army surrender weapons to the toilers)
Capital (meekly)—
Oh, how about the Queen ? She’s looking nettled.
(Queen bridles up)
F. Bulletin—
Well, that, old man, I’ve not exactly settled.
Come blow the pipe, and strike the merry tabor,
And shout —
Everybody— Three cheers for Capital and Labour!
Trio and Chorus.—Cap., Hard., F. Bulletin,
and Everybody.
Capital— This song is not a long ’un,
And it’s certainly the last.
I used to be a wrong ’un
In the days for ever past.
Hardgraft— O, lie used to be a terror,
But he’s altered in a night—
F. Bulletin —We must overlook his error
Now it’s all come right.
Chorus —All come right, all come right;
He was bad at the beginning
Of the yarn we have been spinning,
But he’s sorry for his sinning,
And it’s all come right.
(General breakdown.)

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

The Brentnall-Mabbott criminal libel
action was tried in Brisbane after all. As every-
body expected, the jury returned a verdict of not
guilty, and the “honorable and reverend was
accordingly mulct heavily in costs for which
(once a parson always a parson) a collec-
tion ” is now being made. The verdict,
like that in the Bennett - Stab case m
Svdnev, teaches the lesson that, save m
a case of the direst extremity, the man
who runs a newspaper and takes a libei-actioii
against the fellow who slangs him, is a fool, ine
assailed man should simply use his own columns
to kick back. If he, is too weak or dignified
to do this, he will gain nothing by going to law.
“A newspaper-proprietor who sues a rival news-
paper-proprietor for libel deserves to lose, once
said the astute Sir Samuel Griffith, Q.C., when
consulted by a scribe who meditated issuing a
writ. The costs in the Bennett-STAH case, which
have to be paid by Mr. Bennett, will leave litt e
change out of a cheque for £1200. And for this
the plaintiff gets —what ? Simply a big advt. to
the effect that the Stab called his paper
“Ananias,” and that the jury dicai t think
mattered much.

Reynolds remarks that the appointment of
Dr Randall Davidson, Dean of Windsor, to the
Bishopric of Rochester (which ex-Primate Barry
was fishing for) was a gross job to please Queen
Victoria. The late Dean of Windsor has had no
experience at all of parochial work, whilst the
poor are swarming in his diocese. The history o
Bishop Davidson is almost as entertaining as that
of Canon Duckworth. The latter was tutor in
the Royal family, and for qualities that have not
been publicly specified he was speedily advanced
to his present dignity. The new Bishop s rapio
elevation is due to not dissimilar causes. Me
began his career as domestic chaplain to the late
Dr. Tait, Archbishop of Canterbury, whose
daughter he married. At Oxford he took a third-
rate place, and, until his marriage, was considered
as capable of performing the work, say, of a
curate. But luck in the form of the Queen s
favour stood by him even after the avenues to
promotion seemed closed by the death of his
father-in-law. She sent for the handsome young
chaplain to hear an account of the Primate s last
moments. He came ; he saw ; he conquered. 1 he
Queen soon after presented him to the deanery of
Windsor. Her favour still stands by him, and
he now enjoys, while yet a very young man, the
dignity of being dubbed a Bishop. The emolu-
ments and the jobbery of the Church of England
are alike libels on religion. T he Dean of Windsor
has £2000 a year, with large perquisites and a
luxurious residence. The duties are so light that
the office is practically a sinecure. Canon
Farrar, who has just been appointed successor to
the Rev. Mr. White as Chaplain of the House of
Commons, draws the emoluments of two other
offices—St. Margaret’s Vicarage, £500, and a
oanonry of Westminster, £1000—while, in addi-
tion he derives a good income from the sale of his
works ahme. The death of Dr. Liddon leaves the
oanonry of St. Paul’s vacant. The duties of this
office occupy three months of the year, for the
performance of which a residence is provided and
a salary of £1000 ! What with the Archbishop of
Canterbury’s £15,000 a year, palace, perquisites,
&c., and a host of his colleagues’ enormous dram
ing the clergy of the Church may indeed literally
be poor in spirit, as our Lord taught, but, except-
ing the curates, many of whom are nearly as im-
pecunious as the dockers, they are certainly not
poor in pocket.

A Brisbane journal states that “ there is
an urgent demand for a new blasting com-
pound.” There is at least one newspaper man we
could mention who, if Brisbane people would only
engage him, would be all the blasting compound
they could possibly require for a century. The
name is held over for various reasons, but any-
body who has ever met him at the Melbourne
Herald office, just before press-hour, and been
blasted by him, will require no further particulars.

Hamilton, British officer stationed in
Cairo, was told by the Sheik who owned his
house that he would have to shift, because he was
an immoral man. “Good gracious !” said
Hamilton. “No woman has ever crossed my
threshold.” “That’s just the objection,” said
She : ky, “the man who keeps no harem must
meddie with his neighbour’s.”

A man was found dead beside a water-hole
on Cobran run, near Deniliquin (N.S.W.), the
other day. The police were sent for, came along,
searched the clothes and found nearly watch
and chain, then turned and rode home. Two days
after, a J.P. briefly interviewed the corpse, then
had it shovelled into a shallow hole over which
some logs were dumped down. The dead are
comfortable anywhere ; nevertheless, especially as
the poor fellow left the necessary funds, he should
have been stowed away with the conventional
formahties.

ruptcy Bill proposes to give an insolvency Judg 6

The brightest of eyes must fade sooner or
later, and the truest of loves must pass. Gener-
ally love passes very quickly, and then the angel
with the starlight eyes degenerates into a woman
with an indignant glare and a long milliner’s bill,
and after that marriage is a failure. Sometimes
it is more of a failure than it is on other occasions,
but as a rule it fails considerably. A few years
ago, for instance, a man in South Australia encoun-
tered a thing of hair —a creature all joy and glad-
ness, and he adored her from the top of her glossy
head right down to her two dancing feet, and
asked permission to be her slave for evermore, and
reside with her in a bower or thereabouts. Pre-
sumably she agreed to the bargain, for the other
day howls and groans were heard issuing from an
empty house, and when a policeman proceeded to
investigate he found a woman in an exceedingly
diminished wardrobe chained up to the wall. She
was the gladsome creature already mentioned, and
she was carefully secured in the bower herein-
before alluded to. The place was simply and
plainly ft rnished with a bucket of water and an
old dog’s-hide, and the window was nailed up, and
instead of going to the opera or indulging in other
expensive amusements the bride was accustomed/
to entertain herself cheaply by barking on the
floor. The loving husband who had laid his fond
heart at her feet now threw her victuals at her
head instead, and called round occasionally to
replenish the water-bucket. It was not altogether
what the lady had expected, no doubt, but then
few things in this world are what they seem.
Marriages, as a rule, are a failure, and this one
had failed a little worse than the average—that
was all.
The original genuine article —Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Bishop Stanton, the newly-elect of New-
castle, N.S.W., is greatly given to “enthuse”
over any of the many things which attract his
Argus eye. When he first w'ent to North Queens-
land this little weakness was dreadfully pro-
nounced, and the most commonplace things at
Townsville —the muddy creek, a tin-pot under-
taking establishment, the barren rocks of Melton
Hill, or the blazing climate—evoked such expres-
sions from his lordship as: “Magnificent,”
“beautiful,” “stupendous,” “entrancing,” &c.,
&o. Shortly afterwards the amiable old man
went ’Ome. While in London he tumbled up
against Bob Christison, a N.Q. squatter, and
consequently one of the Bishop’s flock (lambs, we
were going to say, but as events turned
out it was quite the other way on !).
Bob had a scheme in hand for freezing
cattle at Poole Island, Bowen, for the supply
of meat to London, and he explained it fully to
the Bishop, who became quite enthusiastic, and
deeply interested. A company had to be floated,
and lio broker was more anxious to fill the share-
list than the Bishop. At his mission lectures he
advertised the scheme and dragged it in somehow
at the tail-end of every sermon, besides inducing
numbers of his friends to take up I hen
he returned to Townsville, leaving the wily Bob
to complete matters in London. The first sermon
the Bishop delivered after his return was abso-
lutely nothing but an “ad.” or a prospectus of
the scheme. The church was crowded, and the
land-syndicators were powerfully impressed with
the Bishop’s shrewdness in cracking up and
developing the resources of his diocese. Soon
the meat-freezing began, but, after a fearful
outlay, had to be suspended—the thing simply
didn’t pay. In justice to the Bishop, it is but
fair to say that he was hit as hard, or harder, than
any of the other shareholders. Colonial experi-
ence has damped his ardour now, and he turns his
solitary eye on Queensland money-making schemes
with a gleam of anger and suspicion.
